Biography of Abu Hatim al-Tabari al-Qazwini
Ibn Asakir records the biography of Abu Hatim al-Tabari known as al-Qazwini

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the educational journey and scholarly contributions of Abu Hatim al-Tabari al-Qazwini as recorded by Ibn Asakir.

Core Idea
Abu Hatim mastered fiqh, usul, and khilaf through a rigorous chain of teachers.

Ibn Asakir provides a brief but rich biography of one of his own teachers, Abu Hatim al-Tabari al-Qazwini. This passage shows the typical path of a Shafi'i scholar of the time: studying in his hometown, then moving to Baghdad to attend circles of major scholars. It also highlights the integration of Ash'ari theology into fiqh training, as he studied usul al-fiqh under Qadi Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the chain of transmission at the beginning – a common feature in biographical entries.
2
Identify the chain of transmission
Trace the narrators from Ibn Asakir back to al-Shirazi. Who is the immediate source?
The chain is: Ibn Asakir ← al-Samarqandi ← al-Shirazi.
3
List the teachers and subjects studied
Identify each teacher mentioned and the subject they taught Abu Hatim. Write them down in order.
Teachers: shuyukh of Amul (fiqh), Abu Hamid (circle), Ibn al-Laban (farai'd), Qadi Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari (usul).
4
Note what made Abu Hatim special
The passage describes him as 'hafiz of the madhhab and khilaf' and author of many books. Consider what these terms mean.
Hafiz here means he memorized the fiqh rulings, not hadith.
5
Reflect on Ibn Asakir's personal testimony
Ibn Asakir says he did not benefit from anyone as much as from Abu Hatim and Qadi Abu al-Tayyib. Why is this significant?
It shows Abu Hatim's high rank in the eyes of a major historian.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم أبو حاتم الطبرى المعروف بالقزويني رحمه الله كم أخبرنا الشيخ أبو القسم إسماعيل بن أحمد بن السمرقندي قال ثنا الشيخ أبو إسحق إبراهيم بن على الشيرازي لفظا . قال ومنهم شيخنا أبو حاتم محمود بن الحسن الطبري المعروف بالقزويني تفقه بآمل علي شيوخ البلد ثم قدم بغداد وحضر مجلس الشيخ أبي حامد ودرس الفرائض علي الشيخ أبي الحسين بن اللبان وأصول الفقه على القاضي أبي بكر الأشعري رحمه الله ، وكان حافظاً للمذهب والخلاف صنف كتباً كثيرة في الخلاف والمذهب والأصول والجدل ودرس بـبـغـداد وآمل ولم أنتفع بأحد فى الرحلة كما انتفعت به وبالقاضي أبي الطيب الطبرى وتوفى بآمل
And among them is Abu Hatim al-Tabari known as al-Qazwini, may God have mercy on him. The shaykh Abu al-Qasim Isma'il ibn Ahmad al-Samarqandi informed us, saying: Shaykh Abu Ishaq Ibrahim ibn Ali al-Shirazi narrated to us verbally. He said: Among them is our shaykh Abu Hatim Mahmud ibn al-Hasan al-Tabari known as al-Qazwini. He studied fiqh in Amul under the shaykhs of the town, then came to Baghdad and attended the study circle of Shaykh Abu Hamid, and studied the laws of inheritance under Shaykh Abu al-Husayn ibn al-Laban, and usul al-fiqh under Qadi Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari, may God have mercy on him. He was a memorizer of the madhhab and of legal disagreement, he authored many books on legal disagreement, fiqh, usul, and dialectic. He taught in Baghdad and Amul. I did not benefit from anyone in my travels as I benefited from him and from Qadi Abu al-Tayyib al-Tabari, and he died in Amul.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage provides the biography of Abu Hatim al-Tabari al-Qazwini. The chain of transmission goes through al-Shirazi. It describes his education: he began in Amul, then moved to Baghdad where he attended the circle of Abu Hamid al-Isfarayini, studied farai'd (inheritance law) with Ibn al-Laban, and usul al-fiqh with Qadi Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari (the famous Ash'ari theologian). His expertise is highlighted: he memorized the madhhab and khilaf, and wrote numerous works. Ibn Asakir personally testifies that he benefited from him more than any other teacher during his travels, indicating his high rank. He died in Amul. This biography shows the importance of Ash'ari scholars in transmitting both fiqh and usul.

Key Terms
تفقه
فرائض
أصول الفقه
حافظ للمذهب

جدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'حافظاً للمذهب' refers to a hadith memorizer (hafiz).
Correction: In this context, it means he memorized the fiqh rulings of his school (Shafi'i madhhab), not hadith.

Checkpoint

D) Abu al-Tayyib al-Tabari

Key Takeaway
Abu Hatim al-Tabari was a key Shafi'i-Ash'ari scholar who mastered fiqh, usul, and khilaf, and was regarded by Ibn Asakir as his most beneficial teacher.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Abu Hatim studied under multiple teachers across different cities, each specializing in a subject. What subject in your own Islamic learning is you most neglecting, and what is one concrete step you can take this week to seek a qualified teacher or structured resource for it?

Biography of Abu al-Hasan Rasha ibn Nazif

A trusted reciter and judge, Rasha ibn Nazif was the master of Ibn 'Amir's qira'a.

Ibn Asakir records the biography of notable Ash'ari scholars. This passage gives us a glimpse into the life of Rasha ibn Nazif, a renowned Qur'an reciter and judge. Through this short entry, we learn about his death, his character, and his scholarly legacy.

And among them is Abu al-Hasan Rasha ibn Nazif al-Muqri' al-Dimashqi, may God have mercy on him. The shaykh Abu Muhammad ibn al-Akfani al-Amin informed us, saying: Abd al-Aziz ibn Muhammad al-Kattani narrated to us, saying: Our shaykh Abu al-Hasan Rasha ibn Nazif ibn Ma Sha' Allah died on Saturday after the afternoon prayer, the 27th of Muharram of the year 444 AH, and was buried on Sunday. The leadership in the recitation of Ibn 'Amir, may God have mercy on him, ended with him. He recited to Ibn Dawud and others.

Commentary

He is described as trustworthy and reliable, and as having judged correctly. He transmitted hadith from various scholars from Egypt and Iraq. Most notably, he was the leading authority in the qira'a (Qur'anic recitation) of Ibn 'Amir, one of the seven canonical reciters. He studied recitation under Ibn Dawud and others. This biography highlights the importance of Ash'ari scholars in the field of Qur'anic recitation.

Biography of Ibn al-Laban: Genealogy and Scholarly Travels
Ibn Asakir records the biography of Ibn al-Laban al-Isbahani

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical biographical entries preserve lineage and scholarly travel, using Ibn al-Laban as an example.

Core Idea
Genealogy and travel define a scholar's identity in classical Islam.

Ibn Asakir's biographical dictionary is a treasure trove of scholarly lineages and journeys. In this passage, he introduces Ibn al-Laban by tracing his ancestry back to the tribe of Bakr ibn Wail and listing his teachers across three cities. This lesson unpacks the layers of such an entry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the full content and structure.
The text ends abruptly; observe how a typical entry is structured.
2
Examine the genealogy
Notice the long chain of names from Ibn al-Laban back to Bakr ibn Wail. Consider why such detail was recorded.
Genealogy established social and scholarly connections.
3
Identify the travel pattern
List the cities and teachers mentioned: Isfahan (Ibn al-Muqri', etc.), Baghdad (Abu Tahir al-Mukhlis), Mecca (Abu al-Hasan Ahmad). This shows the rihla tradition.
Travel for knowledge (rihla) was a hallmark of classical scholarship.
4
Reflect on the description
Ibn al-Laban is called 'one of the containers of knowledge' and 'a person of religion and virtue'. Discuss what these titles convey.
Such praise phrases are common in biographical entries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم أبو محمد الأصبهاني المعروف بابن اللبان رحمه الله أخبرنا الشيخان أبو الحسن علي بن أحمد الفقيه بدمشق ، وأبو النجم بدر ابن عبد الله الشيحي التاجر ببغداد قالا : قال لنا الشيخ أبو بكر أحمد بن على بن ثابت الخطيب : عبد الله بن محمد بن عبد الرحمن بن أحمد بن عبد الله بن محمد بن النعمان بن عبد السلام بن حبيب بن حطيط بن عقبة بن خثيم بن وائل بن مهانة بن تيم الله بن ثعلبة بن عكابة ابن صعب بن علي بن بكر بن وائل أبو محمد الأصبهاني المعروف بابن اللبان ، أحد أوعية العلم ومن أهل الدين والفضل سمع بأصبهان أبا بكر ابن المقرى وإبراهيم بن عبد الله بن خرشيد قوله وعلي بن محمد بن ابن ميلة وغيرهم، وسمع ببغداد أبا طاهر المخلص وبمكة أبا الحسن أحمد
And among them is Abu Muhammad al-Isbahani known as Ibn al-Laban, may God have mercy on him. The two shaykhs Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Ahmad al-Faqih in Damascus, and Abu al-Najm Badr ibn Abd Allah al-Shayhi al-Tajir in Baghdad said: Shaykh Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ali ibn Thabit al-Khatib said to us: Abd Allah ibn Muhammad ibn Abd al-Rahman ibn Ahmad ibn Abd Allah ibn Muhammad ibn al-Nu'man ibn Abd al-Salam ibn Habib ibn Hitat ibn Uqbah ibn Khuthaym ibn Wail ibn Mahanah ibn Taym Allah ibn Tha'labah ibn Ukabah ibn Sa'b ibn Ali ibn Bakr ibn Wail, Abu Muhammad al-Isbahani known as Ibn al-Laban, one of the containers of knowledge, among the people of religion and virtue. He heard in Isfahan from Abu Bakr ibn al-Muqri', Ibrahim ibn Abd Allah ibn Kharshid Qawluh, Ali ibn Muhammad ibn Ibn Maylah, and others. And he heard in Baghdad from Abu Tahir al-Mukhlis, and in Mecca from Abu al-Hasan Ahmad...
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage gives the full genealogy of Ibn al-Laban, tracing his lineage back to the tribe of Bakr ibn Wail. He is described as 'one of the containers of knowledge' and a person of religion and virtue. His teachers are listed: in Isfahan, he heard from Ibn al-Muqri' and others; in Baghdad from Abu Tahir al-Mukhlis; in Mecca from Abu al-Hasan Ahmad (the text cuts off). This shows his extensive travels in search of knowledge. The entry is part of Ibn Asakir's compilation of Ash'ari scholars, as Ibn al-Laban is a noted scholar. The incomplete ending suggests the original text continues beyond the page.

Key Terms
أوعية العلم
أهل الدين والفضل
سمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The long genealogy is unnecessary and irrelevant to a scholar's identity.
Correction: In classical Islamic scholarship, full lineage was a standard part of identification, helping to distinguish individuals with similar names and to establish tribal and regional affiliations that contextualized a scholar's network.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How can you trace your own 'spiritual lineage' through teachers or scholars you have learned from? Will you take steps to document and honor that connection?
?
The meticulous recording of isnad shows that knowledge was transmitted with care. In what areas of your life do you take such care for accuracy? Where do you cut corners? What would it mean for you to treat your own learning as a sacred trust to be passed on?
?
Ibn al-Laban traveled to three cities seeking knowledge. What is the farthest you have gone in pursuit of truth? Are you willing to travel—physically, intellectually, or spiritually—to learn from those who have greater knowledge? What holds you back?

The Biography of Abu Hatim al-Tabari: A Scholar's Journey
Biography of Abu Hatim al-Tabari by Ibn Asakir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly career of Abu Hatim al-Tabari as recorded by his student Ibn Asakir, and the value of such biographical entries in the Islamic tradition.

Core Idea
Scholarly biographies preserve the chain of knowledge and honor teachers.

Ibn Asakir, the great historian of Damascus, includes in his Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari a biographical entry on his teacher Abu Hatim al-Tabari. This passage not only details Abu Hatim's education and works but also shows the deep respect and gratitude a student has for his teacher. As you read, notice how Ibn Asakir traces his teacher's own teachers and highlights his contributions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, noting the names and places mentioned. Don't worry about understanding every word yet.
Focus on the sequence of events.
2
Trace the scholarly journey
Follow Abu Hatim's path from Amul to Baghdad and back, noting each teacher and subject he studied.
List the subjects: fiqh, fara'id, usul al-fiqh.
3
Reflect on Ibn Asakir's testimony
Pay special attention to the final sentence where Ibn Asakir says he benefited from no one as much as from Abu Hatim. What does this reveal about their relationship?
Consider the adab (etiquette) of a student towards his teacher.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومنهم شيخنا أبو حاتم محمود بن الحسن الطبري المعروف بالقزويني تفقه بآمل علي شيوخ البلد ثم قدم بغداد وحضر مجلس الشيخ أبي حامد ودرس الفرائض علي الشيخ أبي الحسين بن اللبان وأصول الفقه على القاضي أبي بكر الأشعري رحمه الله ، وكان حافظاً للمذهب والخلاف صنف كتباً كثيرة في الخلاف والمذهب والأصول والجدل ودرس ببغداد وآمل ولم أنتفع بأحد فى الرحلة كما انتفعت به وبالقاضي أبي الطيب الطبرى وتوفى بآمل .
He said: Among them is our shaykh Abu Hatim Mahmud ibn al-Hasan al-Tabari, known as al-Qazwini. He studied fiqh in Amul under the shaykhs of the town, then came to Baghdad and attended the circle of Shaykh Abu Hamid, and studied the laws of inheritance under Shaykh Abu al-Husayn ibn al-Laban, and usul al-fiqh under Qadi Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari, may Allah have mercy on him. He was a preserver of the school of law and legal disagreement. He authored many books on disagreement, school of law, principles, and dialectics. He taught in Baghdad and Amul. I have not benefited from anyone in my travels as I benefited from him and from Qadi Abu al-Tayyib al-Tabari. He died in Amul.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry. Ibn Asakir begins by naming his teacher Abu Hatim Mahmud al-Tabari, known as al-Qazwini. He describes his education: he studied fiqh in Amul, then came to Baghdad where he attended the circle of Shaykh Abu Hamid (al-Isfara'ini) and studied inheritance law under Abu al-Husayn ibn al-Laban, and usul al-fiqh under Qadi Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari. He was a specialist in the Shafi'i school and legal disagreement. He authored many books. He also taught. Ibn Asakir then gives a personal testimony: he did not benefit from anyone as much as from Abu Hatim and from Qadi Abu al-Tayyib al-Tabari. This shows the high regard he had for him. The entry ends with the place of death: Amul.

الفرائض
أصول الفقه
الخلاف
المذهب
الجدل

A) Fiqh and hadith

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir's biography of his teacher Abu Hatim al-Tabari exemplifies the Islamic tradition of honoring scholars by recording their teachers, works, and personal impact.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Ibn Asakir testified that he benefited from no one as much as from Abu Hatim and Qadi Abu al-Tayyib. Who has been that transformative person in your life? Have you told them? If not, what is holding you back from expressing that appreciation?

Biography of Rasha ibn Nadif: A Trusted Quran Reciter
Ibn Asakir records the death and scholarly profile of a Quran reciter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key biographical details and scholarly credentials of the Quran reciter Rasha ibn Nadif as recorded by Ibn Asakir.

Core Idea
Rasha ibn Nadif was the final authority on Ibn Amir's recitation.

Ibn Asakir presents a brief yet informative biography of Rasha ibn Nadif, highlighting his death date, his trustworthiness, and his supreme mastery in the qira'a of Ibn Amir. This passage gives us a glimpse into how early Muslim scholars preserved the legacy of Quranic reciters through precise documentation of their lives and chains of learning.

Guided Path
1
Examine the chain of transmission
Read the first part of the passage where Ibn Asakir reports the death date through his own chain. Notice the narrators: Ibn al-Akfani from al-Kattani. This shows how biographical information was transmitted.
The chain indicates the reliability of the report.
2
Identify character descriptions
The passage describes Rasha as 'thiqah ma'mun' (trustworthy and reliable) and mentions his upright conduct. Reflect on how these terms elevate his scholarly status.
These adjectives are key in classical biographical evaluations.
3
Understand his role in Quranic recitation
The passage states that 'leadership in the recitation of Ibn Amir ended with him.' Consider what this implies about his expertise and legacy in the field of qira'at.
This phrase marks him as the highest authority in that recitation tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم أبو الحسن رشا بن نظيف المقري الدمشقى رحمه الله ) أخبرنا الشيخ أبو محمد بن الأكفاني الأمين قال ثنا عبد العزيز بن بن محمد الكتاني ، قال توفى شيخنا أبو الحسن رشا بن نظيف بن ما شاء الله يوم السبت بعد صلاة العصر السابع والعشرين من المحرم سنه أربع وأربعين وأربعماية ودفن يوم الأحد ، وكان ثقة مأموناً قضي علي سداد وأمر جميل ، حدث عن عبد الوهاب بن الحسن بن الوليد الكلابي وغيره من المصريين والعراقيين وغيرهم انتهت إليه الرئاسة في قراءة ابن عامر رحمه الله ، قرأ علي ابن داود وغيره .
Among them is Abu al-Hasan Rasha ibn Nadif al-Muqri al-Dimashqi, may Allah have mercy on him. Shaykh Abu Muhammad ibn al-Akfani al-Amin informed us, saying: ‘Abd al-‘Aziz ibn Muhammad al-Kattani narrated to us, saying: Our shaykh Abu al-Hasan Rasha ibn Nadif ibn Ma Sha’ Allah died on Saturday after the afternoon prayer on the twenty-seventh of Muharram in the year 444 AH, and was buried on Sunday. He was trustworthy and reliable, upright in conduct and excellent in manner. He narrated from ‘Abd al-Wahhab ibn al-Hasan ibn al-Walid al-Kilabi and others from the Egyptians and Iraqis and others. Leadership in the recitation of Ibn ‘Amir, may Allah have mercy on him, ended with him. He recited to Ibn Dawud and others.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry. Ibn Asakir provides the death date and burial of Rasha ibn Nadif, a reciter. He reports via his chain: from Abu Muhammad ibn al-Akfani, from ‘Abd al-‘Aziz al-Kattani, stating the death. Then he describes Rasha as trustworthy and reliable, with good character. He taught many students from Egypt and Iraq. Importantly, he was the final authority on the qira’a of Ibn ‘Amir. He learned from Ibn Dawud and others.

B) Hadith
C) Quranic recitation of Ibn Amir
D) Arabic grammar

Key Takeaway
Rasha ibn Nadif was a trustworthy Quran reciter who became the leading authority on the recitation of Ibn Amir.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you ever pause to consider that every letter you pronounce has been passed down through a chain of trustworthy masters like Rasha ibn Nadif? How does that knowledge change your attentiveness in recitation?
?
Rasha ibn Nadif was known for being 'upright in conduct and excellent in manner.' Is your character matching the weight of the Quran you carry? Which specific trait do you need to work on starting today?
?
The leadership in the recitation of Ibn 'Amir ended with Rasha ibn Nadif—meaning the chain was preserved and passed on. Who are you passing the Quran to? If no one, what concrete step will you take to ensure the recitation continues through you?

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir
beginner

Scholarly Profile of Ibn al-Laban
Ibn Asakir presents the scholarly biography of Ibn al-Laban.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly credentials and character of Ibn al-Laban, as recorded by Ibn Asakir.

Core Idea
Ibn al-Laban was a vessel of knowledge and a model of piety.

This biography introduces the towering scholar Ibn al-Laban, tracing his full lineage and listing his teachers in multiple cities. It highlights his studies in both Ash'ari theology and Shafi'i fiqh, his appointment as judge, and his personal virtues. The passage paints a picture of a man who combined deep learning with asceticism and good character.

Guided Path
1
Read the lineage
Notice the long chain of ancestors, establishing his Arab heritage and connection to the tribe of Bakr ibn Wa'il.
Long lineages were common in biographical writing to honor the scholar.
2
Identify his teachers and studies
List the cities and teachers he studied under, and note the subjects: usul al-diyanat, usul al-fiqh, Shafi'i fiqh, and Quranic readings.
His study under Qadi Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari connects him to the Ash'ari school.
3
Notice his character traits
The passage explicitly mentions several virtues: trustworthiness, excellent recitation, concise debate, piety, worship, asceticism, and good character.
These traits show he was not only learned but also righteous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم أبو محمد الأصبهاني المعروف بابن اللبان رحمه الله أخبرنا الشيخان أبو الحسن علي بن أحمد الفقيه بدمشق ، وأبو النجم بدر ابن عبد الله الشيحي التاجر ببغداد قالا : قال لنا الشيخ أبو بكر أحمد بن على بن ثابت الخطيب : عبد الله بن محمد بن عبد الرحمن بن أحمد بن عبد الله بن محمد بن النعمان بن عبد السلام بن حبیب بن حطيط بن عقبة بن خثيم بن وائل بن مهانة بن تيم الله بن ثعلبة بن عكابة ابن صعب بن علي بن بكر بن وائل أبو محمد الأصبهاني المعروف بابن اللبان ، أحد أوعية العلم ومن أهل الدين والفضل سمع بأصبهان أبا بكر ابن المقرى وإبراهيم بن عبد الله بن خرشيد قوله وعلي بن محمد بن ابن ميلة وغيرهم، وسمع ببغداد أبا طاهر المخلص وبمكة أبا الحسن أحمد بن إبراهيم بن فارس ، وكان ثقة صحب القاضي أبا بكر الأشعرى ودرس عليه أصول الديانات وأصول الفقه ، ودرس فقه الشافعي علي أبي حامد الاسفراینی وقرأ القرآن بعدة روايات ، وولى قضاء إيذج وحدث ببغداد فسمعنا منه وله كتب كثيرة مصنفة وكان من أحسن الناس تلاوة للقران ومن أوجز الناس عبارة فى المناظرة ، مع تدين جميل وعبادة كثيرة وورع بين وتقشف ظاهر وخلق حسن وسمعته يقول حفظت القرآن ولى خمس سنين ، أدرك ابن اللبان شهر رمضان من سنة سبع وعشرين وأربعماية وهو ببغداد فصلي بالناس صلاة التراويح في جميع الشهر ، وكان إذا فرغ من صلاته بالناس في كل ليلة لا يزال قائماً في المسجـد يـصـلى حـتـى يطلع الفجر ، فإذا صلي درس أصحابه وسمعته يقول لم أضع جنبى للنوم في هذا الشهر ليلاً ولا نهارا ، وكان ورده كل ليلة فيما يصلي لنفسه سبعاً من القرآن يقرأه بترتيل وتمهل ولم أر أجود ولا أحسن قراءة منه ، مات بأصبهان في جمادى الآخرة من سنة ست وأربعين وأربعماية ، وسمعت ببغداد من يحكى أن أبا يعلي بن الفراء وأبا محمد التميمي شيخي الحنابلة كانا يقرآن علي أبي محمد بن اللبان الأصول في داره وكل واحد منهما يخفى ذلك عن صاحبه فاجتمعا يوما في دهليزه فقال أحدهما لصاحبه ما جاء بك فقال الذي جاء بك فقال أكتم على وأكتم عليك واتفقا على أن لا يعودا إليه بعد ذلك خوفا أن يطلع عوامهم على حالهما في القراءة عليه
Among them is Abu Muhammad al-Isfahani, known as Ibn al-Laban, may Allah have mercy on him. The two shaykhs, Abu al-Hasan ‘Ali ibn Ahmad the faqih in Damascus and Abu al-Najm Badr ibn ‘Abd Allah al-Shayhi the merchant in Baghdad, informed us, both saying: Shaykh Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn ‘Ali ibn Thabit al-Khatib said to us: ‘Abd Allah ibn Muhammad ibn ‘Abd al-Rahman ibn Ahmad ibn ‘Abd Allah ibn Muhammad ibn al-Nu‘man ibn ‘Abd al-Salam ibn Habib ibn Hutayt ibn ‘Uqba ibn Khuthaym ibn Wa’il ibn Mahanah ibn Taym Allah ibn Tha‘laba ibn ‘Ukaba ibn Sa‘b ibn ‘Ali ibn Bakr ibn Wa’il, Abu Muhammad al-Isfahani, known as Ibn al-Laban, one of the vessels of knowledge and among the people of religion and virtue. He heard in Isfahan from Abu Bakr ibn al-Muqri’ and Ibrahim ibn ‘Abd Allah ibn Khurshid qawlahu and ‘Ali ibn Muhammad ibn Ibn Mila and others, and he heard in Baghdad from Abu Tahir al-Mukhlis and in Mecca from Abu al-Hasan Ahmad ibn Ibrahim ibn Faris. He was trustworthy; he accompanied Qadi Abu Bakr al-Ash‘ari and studied with him the principles of religions and principles of fiqh, and studied Shafi‘i fiqh with Abu Hamid al-Isfara’ini and recited the Quran in multiple readings. He was appointed judge of Idhaj and narrated hadith in Baghdad, and we heard from him. He had many authored books. He was among the best people in Quran recitation and among the most concise people in expression during debate, with excellent religiosity, abundant worship, manifest piety, evident asceticism, and good character. I heard him say: I memorized the Quran when I was five years old. Ibn al-Laban reached Ramadan of the year 427 AH while in Baghdad, and he led the people in tarawih prayer for the entire month. When he finished leading the people in prayer each night, he would remain standing in the mosque praying until dawn. After praying, he would teach his companions. I heard him say: I did not put my side to sleep during this month, night or day. His nightly recitation in his own prayer was seven parts of the Quran, which he recited with slow and measured recitation. I have never seen anyone with better or more beautiful recitation. He died in Isfahan in Jumada al-Akhira of the year 446 AH. I heard in Baghdad from someone who narrated that Abu Ya‘la ibn al-Farra’ and Abu Muhammad al-Tamimi, the two shaykhs of the Hanbalis, used to read usul with Abu Muhammad ibn al-Laban in his house, each hiding that from his companion. They met one day in his hallway and one said to the other, 'What brought you?' He said, 'What brought you.' He said, 'Keep my secret and I'll keep yours.' They agreed not to return to him after that, fearing that their common followers would see their situation in reading with him.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a detailed biographical entry. Ibn Asakir first gives the full lineage of Ibn al-Laban through al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, showing his Arab descent. He describes him as a vessel of knowledge and a person of religion and virtue. Then he lists his teachers in Isfahan, Baghdad, and Mecca. He notes his association with Qadi Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari, studying usul al-diyanat (principles of religions, meaning kalam) and usul al-fiqh. He also studied Shafi'i fiqh with Abu Hamid al-Isfara'ini and learned multiple Quranic readings. He served as judge of Idhaj and taught in Baghdad. Ibn Asakir highlights his excellent character: he was one of the best in Quran recitation and very concise in debate, with piety, worship, asceticism, and good character. A personal testimony: Ibn al-Laban memorized the Quran at age 5. Then an account of his devotion in Ramadan 427 AH: he led tarawih all month, then prayed all night until dawn, never sleeping. His nightly recitation was seven parts (a full hizb, i.e., approximately 1/7 of the Quran) recited slowly. Ibn Asakir attests to his superior recitation. Death in 446 AH. Finally, an anecdote: two prominent Hanbali shaykhs, Abu Ya'la ibn al-Farra' and Abu Muhammad al-Tamimi, secretly studied usul (kalam) with Ibn al-Laban, hiding it from each other. When they discovered their mutual secret, they agreed to keep it and never return, fearing their followers might see them studying Ash'ari theology. This illustrates the sensitivity of studying kalam among Hanbalis at the time.

أصول الديانات
أصول الفقه
القاضي أبا بكر الأشعري
تدين
ورع
تقشف

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Laban was a scholar of deep learning and exemplary character, embodying both knowledge and piety.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn al-Laban was known for concise speech in debate. In your discussions about faith, do you speak to be heard or to serve the truth? Are you willing to be brief and humble, or do you dominate for show?
?
He combined vast learning with abundant worship. Which of these two do you prioritize in your daily life, and what is the danger of neglecting the other? What specific change can you make this week to restore balance?
?
He travelled to multiple cities seeking knowledge. Are you content with what you know, or do you actively seek diverse sources of Islamic learning? If not, what is holding you back from expanding your understanding?

Devotion and the Secret of the Two Hanbali Shaykhs
Ibn Asakir describes Ibn al-Laban's personal devotion and a revealing anecdote.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the depth of Ibn al-Laban's devotional practices and the sensitive nature of studying Ash'ari theology among Hanbalis.

Core Idea
Ibn al-Laban's Ramadan devotion and a secret anecdote reveal tensions around kalam study.

This passage continues the biography with a personal account of Ibn al-Laban's intense devotion during Ramadan, including leading tarawih and praying all night. It ends with a striking anecdote about two prominent Hanbali scholars who secretly studied usul with him, highlighting the theological sensitivities of the time.

Guided Path
1
Read about his early achievement
Note that Ibn al-Laban memorized the Quran at age five, showing his precocious talent.
This is a remarkable feat.
2
Examine his Ramadan practice
He led tarawih all month, then prayed until dawn without sleeping, reciting seven parts of the Quran nightly.
Seven parts refers to a hizb, about 1/7 of the Quran.
3
Understand the anecdote
Two Hanbali shaykhs secretly studied usul with him, each unaware of the other. They agreed to keep it secret and never return.
This shows that studying Ash'ari kalam was controversial among Hanbalis.

He was among the best people in Quran recitation and among the most concise people in expression during debate, with excellent religiosity, abundant worship, manifest piety, evident asceticism, and good character. I heard him say: I memorized the Quran when I was five years old. Ibn al-Laban reached Ramadan of the year 427 AH while in Baghdad, and he led the people in tarawih prayer for the entire month. When he finished leading the people in prayer each night, he would remain standing in the mosque praying until dawn. After praying, he would teach his companions. I heard him say: I did not put my side to sleep during this month, night or day. His nightly recitation in his own prayer was seven parts of the Quran, which he recited with slow and measured recitation. I have never seen anyone with better or more beautiful recitation. He died in Isfahan in Jumada al-Akhira of the year 446 AH. I heard in Baghdad from someone who narrated that Abu Ya‘la ibn al-Farra’ and Abu Muhammad al-Tamimi, the two shaykhs of the Hanbalis, used to read usul with Abu Muhammad ibn al-Laban in his house, each hiding that from his companion. They met one day in his hallway and one said to the other, 'What brought you?' He said, 'What brought you.' He said, 'Keep my secret and I'll keep yours.' They agreed not to return to him after that, fearing that their common followers would see their situation in reading with him.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a detailed biographical entry. Ibn Asakir first gives the full lineage of Ibn al-Laban through al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, showing his Arab descent. He describes him as a vessel of knowledge and a person of religion and virtue. Then he lists his teachers in Isfahan, Baghdad, and Mecca. He notes his association with Qadi Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari, studying usul al-diyanat (principles of religions, meaning kalam) and usul al-fiqh. He also studied Shafi'i fiqh with Abu Hamid al-Isfara'ini and learned multiple Quranic readings. He served as judge of Idhaj and taught in Baghdad. Ibn Asakir highlights his excellent character: he was one of the best in Quran recitation and very concise in debate, with piety, worship, asceticism, and good character. A personal testimony: Ibn al-Laban memorized the Quran at age 5. Then an account of his devotion in Ramadan 427 AH: he led tarawih all month, then prayed all night until dawn, never sleeping. His nightly recitation was seven parts (a full hizb, i.e., approximately 1/7 of the Quran) recited slowly. Ibn Asakir attests to his superior recitation. Death in 446 AH. Finally, an anecdote: two prominent Hanbali shaykhs, Abu Ya'la ibn al-Farra' and Abu Muhammad al-Tamimi, secretly studied usul (kalam) with Ibn al-Laban, hiding it from each other. When they discovered their mutual secret, they agreed to keep it and never return, fearing their followers might see them studying Ash'ari theology. This illustrates the sensitivity of studying kalam among Hanbalis at the time.

سبعاً من القرآن
أبو يعلى بن الفراء
أبو محمد التميمي
أصول

?

Are there areas of your faith where you hide your sincere pursuit of knowledge because of what others might think? Will you let the fear of people prevent you from deepening your understanding of Allah's religion?
?

Biography of Abu Muhammad ibn al-Laban: Scholar and Saintly Figure
Biography with focus on education, piety, and a revealing anecdote.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the life, scholarly achievements, and piety of Abu Muhammad ibn al-Laban, and appreciate how an anecdote reveals sectarian sensitivities of his era.

Core Idea
A scholar of exceptional piety whose knowledge transcended sectarian boundaries.

This passage from Ibn 'Asakir's Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari recounts the life of Abu Muhammad ibn al-Laban, a prominent Ash'ari scholar. It details his education under major figures, his devotion to worship, and a remarkable story about two Hanbali scholars who secretly studied under him. This biography not only shows his personal virtues but also sheds light on the intellectual landscape of 11th-century Baghdad.

Guided Path
1
Read the biographical details
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the lineage, education under Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari and Abu Hamid al-Isfarayini, and his roles as judge and hadith narrator.
Look for the key terms: names of scholars and places.
2
Reflect on his piety
Focus on the description of his Ramadan devotion: leading Tarawih, staying awake all night praying, and reciting a seventh of the Qur'an nightly. Consider what this reveals about his character.
This demonstrates extraordinary devotion.
3
Analyze the anecdote
Consider the story of the two Hanbali scholars who studied under him secretly. Why did they stop? What does this tell us about the scholarly climate?
This anecdote shows respect for his knowledge despite differences.

ابن إبراهيم بن فارس ، وكان ثقة صحب القاضي أبا بكر الأشعرى ودرس عليه أصول الديانات وأصول الفقه ، ودرس فقه الشافعي علي أبي حامد الاسفراینی وقرأ القرآن بعدة روايات ، وولى قضاء إيذج وحدث ببغداد فسمعنا منه وله كتب كثيرة مصنفة وكان من أحسن الناس تلاوة للقران ومن أوجز الناس عبارة فى المناظرة ، مع تدين جميل وعبادة كثيرة وورع بين وتقشف ظاهر وخلق حسن وسمعته يقول حفظت القرآن ولى خمس سنين ، أدرك ابن اللبان شهر رمضان من سنة سبع وعشرين وأربعماية وهو ببغداد فصلي بالناس صلاة التراويح في جميع الشهر ، وكان إذا فرغ من صلاته بالناس في كل ليلة لا يزال قائماً في المسجـد يـصـلى حـتـى يطلع الفجر ، فإذا صلي درس أصحابه وسمعته يقول لم أضع جنبى للنوم في هذا الشهر ليلاً ولا نهارا ، وكان ورده كل ليلة فيما يصلي لنفسه سبعاً من القرآن يقرأه بترتيل وتمهل ولم أر أجود ولا أحسن قراءة منه ، مات بأصبهان في جمادى الآخرة من سنة ست وأربعين وأربعماية ، وسمعت ببغداد من يحكى أن أبا يعلي بن الفراء وأبا محمد التميمي شيخي الحنابلة كانا يقرآن علي أبي محمد بن اللبان الأصول في داره وكل واحد منهما يخفى ذلك عن صاحبه فاجتمعا يوما في دهليزه فقال أحدهما لصاحبه ما جاء بك فقال الذي جاء بك فقال أكتم على وأكتم عليك واتفقا على أن لا يعودا إليه بعد ذلك خوفا أن يطلع عوامهم على حالهما في القراءة عليه.
Ibn Ibrahim ibn Faris, and he was trustworthy. He accompanied the judge Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari and studied under him the principles of religions and principles of jurisprudence, and he studied the jurisprudence of al-Shafi'i under Abu Hamid al-Isfarayini, and he recited the Qur'an with multiple recitations. He was appointed judge of Idhaj and narrated hadith in Baghdad, and we heard from him. He had many authored books, and he was among the best of people in recitation of the Qur'an and among the most concise of people in expression during debate, with beautiful religiosity, abundant worship, evident scrupulousness, apparent asceticism, and good character. I heard him say: 'I memorized the Qur'an when I was five years old.' Ibn al-Laban experienced Ramadan of the year 427 AH while in Baghdad, and he led the people in Tarawih prayer throughout the month. When he finished leading the people in prayer each night, he would remain standing in the mosque praying until dawn. When he prayed, he would teach his companions. I heard him say: 'I did not lay my side for sleep during this month, night or day.' His daily recitation (ward) every night in his personal prayer was a seventh of the Qur'an, which he recited with slow, measured recitation. I did not see anyone with better or more beautiful recitation than him. He died in Isfahan in Jumada al-Akhira of the year 446 AH. And I heard in Baghdad from someone who related that Abu Ya'la ibn al-Farra' and Abu Muhammad al-Tamimi, the two Hanbali shaykhs, used to read under Abu Muhammad ibn al-Laban the principles (usul) in his house, each of them concealing that from the other. They met one day in his hallway; one said to the other: 'What brought you?' He said: 'The same that brought you.' He said: 'I conceal from you, and you conceal from me.' They agreed not to return to him after that, fearing that their common folk would find out about their studying under him.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents a biographical account of Abu Muhammad ibn al-Laban, focusing on his scholarly achievements and extraordinary piety. It begins by establishing his lineage and trustworthiness, then details his education under prominent scholars: he studied the principles of religion and jurisprudence with Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari, and Shafi'i jurisprudence with Abu Hamid al-Isfarayini. He also mastered multiple Qur'anic recitations. His professional roles included serving as judge of Idhaj and narrating hadith in Baghdad. The author emphasizes his excellent character, describing him as having beautiful religiosity, abundant worship, and remarkable conciseness in debate. A notable personal detail is that he memorized the Qur'an at age five. The passage then highlights his devotion during Ramadan of 427 AH: he led Tarawih prayers all month, and after each night's prayer he would remain standing in the mosque praying until dawn, then teach his companions. He claimed not to have slept at all during that month. His nightly personal recitation (ward) was a seventh of the Qur'an, recited slowly and beautifully. The author states he never saw anyone with better recitation. After noting his death in 446 AH, the passage concludes with an anecdote illustrating his reputation among Hanbali scholars: Abu Ya'la ibn al-Farra' and Abu Muhammad al-Tamimi secretly used to study under him, but when they discovered each other's visits, they agreed to stop out of fear that their followers would learn of their association with an Ash'ari scholar. This shows both his scholarly stature and the sectarian sensitivities of the time.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The anecdote is meant to criticize the Hanbali scholars for deception.
Correction: The anecdote is not meant to criticize them, but to show the high regard for Ibn al-Laban's knowledge despite sectarian divisions.

The Accidental Scholar: Sulaym al-Razi's Encounter with Abu Hamid
Sulaym recounts his journey and chance encounter with Abu Hamid al-Isfarayini.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sulaym al-Razi's chance encounter with Abu Hamid al-Isfarayini led him to dedicate himself to fiqh.

Core Idea
A random detour sparked a lifelong commitment to Islamic jurisprudence.

This lesson narrates the unexpected turning point in Sulaym al-Razi's youth. Originally seeking language studies, he stumbled upon a fiqh lecture that captivated him. The passage shows how a single lesson can redirect a life.

Guided Path
1
Read the story of his entry into Baghdad
Sulaym recounts his initial aim to study language and his routine of visiting a shaykh. Notice the casual beginning.
2
Notice the chance encounter
He was told the shaykh was in the bathhouse and on the way he passed by Abu Hamid al-Isfarayini who was dictating. This detour changed his path.
3
Observe his reaction
He entered the mosque, heard the lesson on the Book of Fasting, admired it, and decided to complete the book. He then attached himself to Abu Hamid and wrote down all his commentaries.
This shows his immediate dedication.

ومنهم أبو الفتح سليم بن أيوب الرازي رحمه الله ) حدثنا الشيخ الفقيه أبو الحسن على بن المسلم من لفظه قال : حدثني أبو نصر أحمد بن محمد بن سعيد الطريثيثي قال سمعت الفقيه سليماً رحمه الله يقول : دخلت بغداد في حداثتي أطلب علم اللغة فكنت آتی شيخاً ذكره فبكرت في بعض الأيام فقيل لى هو في الحمام فمضيت نحوه فعبرت في طريقي على الشيخ أبي حامد الاسفرايني وهو يملى ، فدخلت المسجد وجلست مع الطلبة فوجدته في كتاب الصيام في هذه المسئلة إذا ولج ثم أحس بالفجر فنزع فاستحسنت ذلك وعلقت الدرس على فلما عدت إلى منزلى وجعلت أعيد الدرس حلالي وقلت كان ظهر جزء معی أتم هذا الكتاب يعنى كتاب الصيام فعلقت كتاب الصيام ولزمت الشيخ أبا حامد حتى علقت عليه جميع التعليق قال وسمعت أبا نصر يقول سمعت سليماً يقول : وضعت منى صور ورفعت من أبي الحسن بن المحاملي بغداد . قرأت بخط شيخنا أبي الفرج غيث بن على بن عبد السلام التنوخي الصورى غرق أبو الفتح سليم بن أيوب بن سليم الرازي في بحر القلزم عند ساحل جدة بعد عوده من الحج في صفر سنة سبع وأربعين ، وكان قد نيف على الثمانين ، حدثني بذلك ابنه إبراهيم وكان فقيهاً جيداً مشاراً إليه في علمه ، صنف الكثير فى الفقه وغيره ودرس وحدث عن أبي حامد الاسفراینی و غیره . حدثنا عنه جماعة وهو أول من نشر هذا العلم بصور وانتفع به جماعة وكان أحد من تفقه عليه بها الفقيه أبو الفتح نصر بن إبراهيم المقدسى ، وحدثت عنه أنه كان يحاسب نفسه على الانفاس لا يدع وقتا عليه بغير فائدة إما ينسخ أو يدرس أو يقرأ أو ينسخ شيئاً كثيراً يمضى لقد حدثني عنه شيخنا أبو الفرج الاسفرايني أنه نزل يوماً إلى داره ورجع فقال قد قرأت جزءاً في طريقي قال وحدثني المؤمل بن الحسن أنه رأى سليماً حفى عليه القلم فإلى أن قطه جعل يحرك شفتيه فعلم أنه يقرأ بازاء إصلاحه القلم لئلا يمضى عليه زمان وهو فارغ أو كما قال .
And among them is Abu al-Fath Sulaym ibn Ayyub al-Razi (may Allah have mercy on him). The shaykh the jurist Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn al-Muslim narrated to us from his dictation, saying: Abu Nasr Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Sa'id al-Tarithithi narrated to me, saying: I heard the jurist Sulaym (may Allah have mercy on him) say: I entered Baghdad in my youth seeking knowledge of language. I used to come to a certain shaykh, and one day I went early and was told he was in the bathhouse. So I went towards him, and on my way I passed by Shaykh Abu Hamid al-Isfarayini who was dictating. I entered the mosque and sat with the students, and I found him in the Book of Fasting discussing this issue: 'If one enters, then feels dawn, then withdraws.' I admired that and wrote down the lesson. When I returned home, I began to review the lesson [...] and I said: 'I have a volume with me; I will complete this book' – meaning the Book of Fasting. So I wrote down the Book of Fasting and attached myself to Shaykh Abu Hamid until I had written down all his commentaries. He said: And I heard Abu Nasr say: I heard Sulaym say: '[...]'. I read in the handwriting of our shaykh Abu al-Faraj Ghayth ibn Ali ibn Abd al-Salam al-Tanukhi al-Suri: Abu al-Fath Sulaym ibn Ayyub ibn Sulaym al-Razi drowned in the Red Sea near the coast of Jeddah after returning from Hajj in Safar of the year 447 AH. He was over eighty years old. His son Ibrahim told me that, and he was a good jurist, well-regarded in his knowledge. He authored many works in fiqh and other fields, taught, and narrated from Abu Hamid al-Isfarayini and others. Many narrated from him, and he was the first to spread this knowledge in Sur, and many benefited from him. Among those who studied fiqh with him there was the jurist Abu al-Fath Nasr ibn Ibrahim al-Maqdisi. It was related to me about him that he used to hold himself accountable for every breath, not leaving any time without benefit – either copying, teaching, reading, or copying something quickly. Our shaykh Abu al-Faraj al-Isfarayini narrated to me about him that he once went down to his house and returned, saying: 'I read a part (juz') on my way.' He said: And al-Mu'ammal ibn al-Hasan narrated to me that he saw Sulaym with a pen that had become blunt; while he was sharpening it, he moved his lips, so he knew that he was reading in return for sharpening his pen, lest time pass while he was idle – or as he said.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents a biographical account of Abu al-Fath Sulaym ibn Ayyub al-Razi. It begins with a chain of narration leading to Sulaym himself, who recounts his early quest for knowledge in Baghdad. Originally seeking language studies, he accidentally encountered Shaykh Abu Hamid al-Isfarayini dictating a lesson on the Book of Fasting, specifically discussing a scenario about a person performing a ritual and sensing dawn. Sulaym was impressed, wrote down the lesson, and later decided to complete the entire Book of Fasting under Abu Hamid. He became a dedicated student, writing down all his commentaries. The narrative then shifts to a note about Sulaym's death: he drowned in the Red Sea near Jeddah after returning from Hajj in 447 AH at over eighty years old. The author provides this information from his son Ibrahim and from the handwriting of Shaykh Abu al-Faraj al-Tanukhi. Sulaym is described as a prolific author and teacher who first spread this knowledge in the city of Sur, with many benefiting from his teaching. Notably, the jurist Abu al-Fath Nasr ibn Ibrahim al-Maqdisi was one of his students. The passage then highlights his extreme discipline: he held himself accountable for every breath, using every moment for copying, teaching, reading, or other beneficial activity. Two anecdotes illustrate this: one where he read a full juz' on the way to his house, and another where while sharpening a blunt pen, he moved his lips reading so that no time would be wasted. This biography emphasizes his relentless dedication to learning and productivity.

Key Terms
سليم بن أيوب الرازي
أبو حامد الاسفرايني
كتاب الصيام

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sulaym took a detour to a bathhouse but ended up in a lecture that changed his life. What 'bathhouse'—a mundane or routine task—are you heading toward today? What might happen if you allowed yourself a small detour to something spiritually or intellectually enriching? Will you take it?
?
When Sulaym heard the lecture, he immediately copied it and later resolved to complete the book. Think of the last time you heard a powerful talk or read something that moved you. Did you take any tangible action afterward? If not, what held you back from committing?
?
Sulaym attached himself to Abu Hamid 'until he wrote down all his commentaries.' Is there a teacher or scholar whose works you could commit to studying systematically? What is stopping you from making that dedication starting today?

The Breath-Counting Scholar: Sulaym al-Razi's Discipline and Death
Account of Sulaym's death, his scholarly influence, and his meticulous use of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extreme dedication and time-consciousness of Sulaym al-Razi, as well as the details of his death.

Core Idea
Sulaym counted every breath as an opportunity for knowledge.

After establishing his credentials, the biography shifts to Sulaym's final years and his remarkable character. He was known for maximizing every moment, even while sharpening a pen. This lesson presents anecdotes that illustrate his relentless pursuit of beneficial activity.

Guided Path
1
Read about his death
The passage reports that he drowned in the Red Sea returning from Hajj at over eighty. This gives a sense of his age and the end of his life.
Note the precise date: Safar 447 AH.
2
Learn about his scholarly legacy
He was the first to spread knowledge in Sur, taught many, and authored many works. His student Abu al-Fath Nasr ibn Ibrahim al-Maqdisi is highlighted.
3
Examine his time management
He held himself accountable for every breath, never wasting time. Examples: reading a juz' on the way home and reading while sharpening a pen.
These anecdotes show his intense discipline.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم أبو الفتح سليم بن أيوب الرازي رحمه الله ) حدثنا الشيخ الفقيه أبو الحسن على بن المسلم من لفظه قال : حدثني أبو نصر أحمد بن محمد بن سعيد الطريثيثي قال سمعت الفقيه سليماً رحمه الله يقول : دخلت بغداد في حداثتي أطلب علم اللغة فكنت آتی شيخاً ذكره فبكرت في بعض الأيام فقيل لى هو في الحمام فمضيت نحوه فعبرت في طريقي على الشيخ أبي حامد الاسفرايني وهو يملى ، فدخلت المسجد وجلست مع الطلبة فوجدته في كتاب الصيام في هذه المسئلة إذا ولج ثم أحس بالفجر فنزع فاستحسنت ذلك وعلقت الدرس على فلما عدت إلى منزلى وجعلت أعيد الدرس حلالي وقلت كان ظهر جزء معی أتم هذا الكتاب يعنى كتاب الصيام فعلقت كتاب الصيام ولزمت الشيخ أبا حامد حتى علقت عليه جميع التعليق قال وسمعت أبا نصر يقول سمعت سليماً يقول : وضعت منى صور ورفعت من أبي الحسن بن المحاملي بغداد . قرأت بخط شيخنا أبي الفرج غيث بن على بن عبد السلام التنوخي الصورى غرق أبو الفتح سليم بن أيوب بن سليم الرازي في بحر القلزم عند ساحل جدة بعد عوده من الحج في صفر سنة سبع وأربعين ، وكان قد نيف على الثمانين ، حدثني بذلك ابنه إبراهيم وكان فقيهاً جيداً مشاراً إليه في علمه ، صنف الكثير فى الفقه وغيره ودرس وحدث عن أبي حامد الاسفراینی و غیره . حدثنا عنه جماعة وهو أول من نشر هذا العلم بصور وانتفع به جماعة وكان أحد من تفقه عليه بها الفقيه أبو الفتح نصر بن إبراهيم المقدسى ، وحدثت عنه أنه كان يحاسب نفسه على الانفاس لا يدع وقتا عليه بغير فائدة إما ينسخ أو يدرس أو يقرأ أو ينسخ شيئاً كثيراً يمضى لقد حدثني عنه شيخنا أبو الفرج الاسفرايني أنه نزل يوماً إلى داره ورجع فقال قد قرأت جزءاً في طريقي قال وحدثني المؤمل بن الحسن أنه رأى سليماً حفى عليه القلم فإلى أن قطه جعل يحرك شفتيه فعلم أنه يقرأ بازاء إصلاحه القلم لئلا يمضى عليه زمان وهو فارغ أو كما قال .
And among them is Abu al-Fath Sulaym ibn Ayyub al-Razi (may Allah have mercy on him). The shaykh the jurist Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn al-Muslim narrated to us from his dictation, saying: Abu Nasr Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Sa'id al-Tarithithi narrated to me, saying: I heard the jurist Sulaym (may Allah have mercy on him) say: I entered Baghdad in my youth seeking knowledge of language. I used to come to a certain shaykh, and one day I went early and was told he was in the bathhouse. So I went towards him, and on my way I passed by Shaykh Abu Hamid al-Isfarayini who was dictating. I entered the mosque and sat with the students, and I found him in the Book of Fasting discussing this issue: 'If one enters, then feels dawn, then withdraws.' I admired that and wrote down the lesson. When I returned home, I began to review the lesson [...] and I said: 'I have a volume with me; I will complete this book' – meaning the Book of Fasting. So I wrote down the Book of Fasting and attached myself to Shaykh Abu Hamid until I had written down all his commentaries. He said: And I heard Abu Nasr say: I heard Sulaym say: '[...]'. I read in the handwriting of our shaykh Abu al-Faraj Ghayth ibn Ali ibn Abd al-Salam al-Tanukhi al-Suri: Abu al-Fath Sulaym ibn Ayyub ibn Sulaym al-Razi drowned in the Red Sea near the coast of Jeddah after returning from Hajj in Safar of the year 447 AH. He was over eighty years old. His son Ibrahim told me that, and he was a good jurist, well-regarded in his knowledge. He authored many works in fiqh and other fields, taught, and narrated from Abu Hamid al-Isfarayini and others. Many narrated from him, and he was the first to spread this knowledge in Sur, and many benefited from him. Among those who studied fiqh with him there was the jurist Abu al-Fath Nasr ibn Ibrahim al-Maqdisi. It was related to me about him that he used to hold himself accountable for every breath, not leaving any time without benefit – either copying, teaching, reading, or copying something quickly. Our shaykh Abu al-Faraj al-Isfarayini narrated to me about him that he once went down to his house and returned, saying: 'I read a part (juz') on my way.' He said: And al-Mu'ammal ibn al-Hasan narrated to me that he saw Sulaym with a pen that had become blunt; while he was sharpening it, he moved his lips, so he knew that he was reading in return for sharpening his pen, lest time pass while he was idle – or as he said.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents a biographical account of Abu al-Fath Sulaym ibn Ayyub al-Razi. It begins with a chain of narration leading to Sulaym himself, who recounts his early quest for knowledge in Baghdad. Originally seeking language studies, he accidentally encountered Shaykh Abu Hamid al-Isfarayini dictating a lesson on the Book of Fasting, specifically discussing a scenario about a person performing a ritual and sensing dawn. Sulaym was impressed, wrote down the lesson, and later decided to complete the entire Book of Fasting under Abu Hamid. He became a dedicated student, writing down all his commentaries. The narrative then shifts to a note about Sulaym's death: he drowned in the Red Sea near Jeddah after returning from Hajj in 447 AH at over eighty years old. The author provides this information from his son Ibrahim and from the handwriting of Shaykh Abu al-Faraj al-Tanukhi. Sulaym is described as a prolific author and teacher who first spread this knowledge in the city of Sur, with many benefiting from his teaching. Notably, the jurist Abu al-Fath Nasr ibn Ibrahim al-Maqdisi was one of his students. The passage then highlights his extreme discipline: he held himself accountable for every breath, using every moment for copying, teaching, reading, or other beneficial activity. Two anecdotes illustrate this: one where he read a full juz' on the way to his house, and another where while sharpening a blunt pen, he moved his lips reading so that no time would be wasted. This biography emphasizes his relentless dedication to learning and productivity.

Key Terms
بحر القلزم
أبو الفتح نصر بن إبراهيم المقدسي
صور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that scholarly productivity only happens in formal study sessions.
Correction: Sulaym's example shows that beneficial activities can fill every moment, even walking or sharpening a pen.

Key Takeaway
Sulaym al-Razi's life exemplifies maximizing every moment for knowledge, even while performing mundane tasks like sharpening a pen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you go about your daily routines—commuting, waiting in line, or doing household chores—do you use those moments for beneficial learning or remembrance? If you were held accountable for every breath today, how many would find you idle? What is one specific change you will make tomorrow to reclaim a wasted moment for knowledge?
?
The scholar sharpened his pen while reading so that no time passed without benefit. Examine your own habits: are there small pockets of time you currently fill with distraction (scrolling, staring, worrying) that could be turned into moments of growth? Write down one concrete plan to convert five minutes of ‘empty’ time into something meaningful.
?
If you truly believed that every breath will be accounted for as Sulaym believed, what would you do differently right now? Is your current use of time a reflection of that belief, or is it otherwise? Commit to one action today that shows you value each breath as a gift for seeking good.

The Devout Scholar: Al-Khabbazi
Biography of al-Khabbazi highlighting his recitation and piety

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the life and character of Abu Abd Allah al-Khabbazi, focusing on his scholarly reliability and profound nightly worship.

Core Idea
Piety and scholarship: al-Khabbazi's night worship made his supplications answered.

This lesson presents a brief yet inspiring biography from Ibn 'Asakir's Tabyin. We learn about a reciter known for his nightly devotion, whose copy of a hadith work was considered authoritative in his time. His life offers a model of combining scholarship with deep piety.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the key facts about al-Khabbazi's life, death, and habits.
Pay attention to the phrase 'reliance in his time was on his hearing and his copy'.
2
Identify the biographical details
Note his full name, death date, who led his funeral, and his journey to hear from al-Kashmihani.
These details establish his scholarly credentials.
3
Reflect on his nightly worship
Consider the phrase 'he used to enliven the night with recitation, supplication, and weeping' and what it tells us about his piety.
This was so renowned that his supplication was believed to be answered.
4
Consider the legacy statement
Think about the closing statement 'no one like him was seen after him'. What does this convey about the author's view?
It highlights his exceptional status in both knowledge and worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم أبو عبد الله الخبازى المقرى النيسابوري رحمه الله ) كتب إلى الشيخ أبو الحسن عبد الغافر بن إسماعيل الفارسي قال : الله فسمعه محمد ابن علی بن محمد بن الحسن الأستاذ الإمام المقرى أبو عبد الخبازی ، توفى في شهر رمضان سنة سبع وأربعين وأربعماية وصلى عليه أبا عثمان ورحل إلى الكشميهني لسماع الصحيح الصابونی یعنی وقرىء عليه ، وكان الاعتماد في وقته على سماعه ونسخته وكان يحيى الليل بالقراءة والدعاء والبكاء حتى قيل أنه كان مستجاب الدعوة لم ير بعده مثله .
And among them is Abu Abd Allah al-Khabbazi al-Muqri al-Naysaburi (may Allah have mercy on him). He wrote to Shaykh Abu al-Hasan Abd al-Ghafir ibn Isma'il al-Farsi, saying: 'Allah heard him.' Muhammad ibn Ali ibn Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, the master teacher, the reciter Abu Abd al-Khabbazi died in the month of Ramadan of the year 447 AH, and Abu Uthman prayed over him. He traveled to al-Kashmihani to hear the Sahih of al-Sabuni – meaning it was recited to him. Reliance in his time was on his hearing and his copy. He used to enliven the night with recitation, supplication, and weeping, until it was said that his supplication was answered; no one like him was seen after him.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage provides a brief biography of Abu Abd Allah al-Khabbazi al-Muqri al-Naysaburi. It begins with a note that he wrote to Shaykh Abu al-Hasan Abd al-Ghafir al-Farsi, but the text after 'saying' is unclear ('Allah heard him' may be part of the letter or a remark). The core information is given: his full name as Muhammad ibn Ali ibn Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, his title 'al-Muqri' (the Qur'an reciter), and his death in Ramadan 447 AH, with Abu Uthman leading his funeral prayer. He traveled to al-Kashmihani (a scholar) to hear the Sahih of al-Sabuni (a well-known hadith collection). The passage emphasizes that in his time, reliance was placed on his hearing and his copy of the text. He was known for his nightly devotion: he spent nights reciting the Qur'an, making supplication, and weeping, to the extent that it was believed his supplications were answered. The biography concludes by stating that no one like him was seen after him, highlighting his exceptional piety and reputation.

Correction: The phrase is ambiguous and likely part of an interrupted letter; it does not necessarily indicate a direct divine statement.

?

The Discipline of a Scholar: Sulaym al-Razi
Ibn 'Asakir narrates the death and scholarly discipline of Sulaym al-Razi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly dedication and discipline of Sulaym al-Razi as described by Ibn 'Asakir.

Core Idea
Scholars accounted for every breath; a model of time management.

Ibn 'Asakir presents the life of Sulaym al-Razi not just through his death but through his relentless pursuit of knowledge. The passage shows how he used every moment for learning, even while walking or trimming a pen. This lesson explores the biography of a scholar who made every second count.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full translation to grasp the narrative flow.
Pay attention to the anecdotes about his time management.
2
Identify key biographical details
Note the date and manner of his death, his age, and his scholarly achievements such as authoring books and teaching.
He drowned near Jeddah after Hajj in 447 AH.
3
Understand 'muhasabat al-anfas'
The passage says he would account for his breaths, never letting a moment pass without benefit. Reflect on what that means for a scholar's life.
This is a central theme of the biography.
4
Reflect on personal application
Consider how you can apply Sulaym's discipline to your own studies, even in small moments like walking or waiting.
The anecdotes illustrate using even pen-trimming time for recitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قرأت بخط شيخنا أبي الفرج غيث بن على بن عبد السلام التنوخي الصورى غرق أبو الفتح سليم بن أيوب بن سليم الرازي في بحر القلزم عند ساحل جدة بعد عوده من الحج في صفر سنة سبع وأربعين ، وكان قد نيف على الثمانين ، حدثني بذلك ابنه إبراهيم وكان فقيهاً جيداً مشاراً إليه في علمه ، صنف الكثير فى الفقه وغيره ودرس وحدث عن أبي حامد الاسفراینی و غیره . حدثنا عنه جماعة وهو أول من نشر هذا العلم بصور وانتفع به جماعة وكان أحد من تفقه عليه بها الفقيه أبو الفتح نصر بن إبراهيم المقدسى ، وحدثت عنه أنه كان يحاسب نفسه على الانفاس لا يدع وقتا عليه بغير فائدة إما ينسخ أو يدرس أو يقرأ أو ينسخ شيئاً كثيراً يمضى لقد حدثني عنه شيخنا أبو الفرج الاسفرايني أنه نزل يوماً إلى داره ورجع فقال قد قرأت جزءاً في طريقي قال وحدثني المؤمل بن الحسن أنه رأى سليماً حفى عليه القلم فإلى أن قطه جعل يحرك شفتيه فعلم أنه يقرأ بازاء إصلاحه القلم لئلا يمضى عليه زمان وهو فارغ أو كما قال.
I read in the handwriting of our shaykh Abu al-Faraj Ghayth ibn 'Ali ibn 'Abd al-Salam al-Tanukhi al-Suri: Abu al-Fath Sulaym ibn Ayyub ibn Sulaym al-Razi drowned in the sea of Qulzum near the coast of Jeddah after his return from Hajj in Safar of the year 447, and he had exceeded eighty. His son Ibrahim told me that, and he was a good jurist distinguished in his knowledge. He authored many works in fiqh and other subjects, taught, and narrated from Abu Hamid al-Isfara'ini and others. A group narrated from us, and he was the first to spread this knowledge in Sur, and a group benefited from him. Among those who studied fiqh under him there was the jurist Abu al-Fath Nasr ibn Ibrahim al-Maqdisi. It was reported from him that he would account for his breaths, not leaving any time without benefit, either copying, teaching, reading, or copying something quickly. Our shaykh Abu al-Faraj al-Isfara'ini told me about him: one day he went down to his house and returned, saying: 'I read a juz' on my way.' Al-Mu'ammal ibn al-Hasan told me that he saw Sulaym with a pen that needed trimming, and while he trimmed it, he moved his lips, so he knew he was reading while fixing the pen so that no time would pass without him being occupied, or as he said.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a biographical notice from Ibn 'Asakir about Abu al-Fath Sulaym ibn Ayyub al-Razi. It begins with a report from Ibn 'Asakir's shaykh Abu al-Faraj about Sulaym's death: he drowned in the Red Sea (Bahr al-Qulzum) near Jeddah after returning from Hajj in Safar 447 AH, at over eighty years old. His son Ibrahim, a respected jurist, conveyed this. Then the passage lists Sulaym's achievements: he authored many works in fiqh and other fields, taught, and narrated hadith. He was the first to spread 'this knowledge' (likely fiqh or hadith) in the city of Sur, and many benefited from him. A specific student, Abu al-Fath Nasr al-Maqdisi, is named. The passage then describes his exceptional discipline: he would hold himself accountable for every breath, never letting a moment pass without benefit—he would copy, teach, read, or quickly write something. An anecdote from Abu al-Faraj al-Isfara'ini illustrates this: once Sulaym went home and returned, having read an entire juz' on the way. Another anecdote from al-Mu'ammal ibn al-Hasan shows that even while trimming a pen, he would move his lips in recitation to avoid idleness. The passage emphasizes his scrupulous use of time, a model of scholarly diligence.

Misconception: Sulaym's drowning indicates a lack of divine favor or a punishment.
Correction: The passage presents his death as a historical fact without any negative judgment; many righteous scholars died in accidents. The focus is on his virtuous life and time management.

?

Steadfastness of al-Khazzazi During Persecution
Ibn 'Asakir narrates al-Khazzazi's steadfastness during the mihnah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Khazzazi's response to religious persecution exemplifies steadfast faith and strength of certainty.

Core Idea
True certainty leads to patient perseverance, not flight or compromise.

Ibn 'Asakir presents the biography of Abu 'Abd Allah al-Khazzazi, focusing on his conduct during a notorious trial known as the mihnah of al-Kunduri. While many scholars either fled or renounced their school under pressure, al-Khazzazi chose a third path: he remained in his home, silent and steadfast, until his death. The passage invites us to reflect on the relationship between certitude (yaqin) and action in times of trial.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the two main sections: his scholarly life and his response to the trial.
The passage uses a chain of transmission; the core lesson is in the second part.
2
Identify his actions vs. others'
Contrast the responses of the other scholars (some fled, some renounced) with al-Khazzazi's refusal to leave or renounce.
3
Reflect on the key qualities
The passage emphasizes 'strength of his certainty' (quwwat yaqinihi) and patience (sabiran). Consider how these qualities underpin his action.
Certainty here is not just intellectual but deeply transformative.
4
Connect to the broader lesson
Ask yourself: What does this story teach about the relationship between strong faith and outward conduct in times of pressure?
The lesson is that steadfastness is a practical fruit of yaqin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتب إلى الشيخ أبو الحسن عبد الغافر بن إسماعيل الفارسي قال : فسمعه محمد ابن علی بن محمد بن الحسن الأستاذ الإمام المقرى أبو عبد الخبازی ، توفى في شهر رمضان سنة سبع وأربعين وأربعماية وصلى عليه أبا عثمان ورحل إلى الكشميهني لسماع الصحيح الصابونی یعنی وقرىء عليه ، وكان الاعتماد في وقته على سماعه ونسخته وكان يحيى الليل بالقراءة والدعاء والبكاء حتى قيل أنه كان مستجاب الدعوة لم ير بعده مثله . سمعت الشيخ أبا المحاسن عبد الرزاق بن محمد الطبسي بنيسابور يحكى عن بعض مشايخه أنه لما امتحن أصحابنا بنيسابور في أيام الكندرى ، كان فيهم من خرج عن البلد ، وفيهم من أجاب إلى التبرى من المذهب وأن الخبازى أمتنع من الإجابة ولم يخرج من البلد ولازم بيته إلى أن مات صابراً على دينه معتصماً بقوة يقينه
Wrote to me Shaykh Abu al-Hasan 'Abd al-Ghafir ibn Isma'il al-Farsi, saying: I heard Muhammad ibn 'Ali ibn Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, the teacher and imam, the reciter, Abu 'Abd Allah al-Khazzazi; he died in Ramadan of the year 447 AH, and Abu 'Uthman prayed over him. He traveled to al-Kashmihani to hear the Sahih of al-Sabuni, and it was recited to him. Reliance in his time was on his hearing and his copy. He would keep the night alive with recitation, supplication, and weeping, so much so that it was said his supplication was answered. None like him was seen after him. I heard Shaykh Abu al-Mahasin 'Abd al-Razzaq ibn Muhammad al-Tabasi in Nishapur relate from some of his shaykhs: when our companions in Nishapur were tested in the days of al-Kunduri, among them were those who left the city, and among them were those who agreed to disavow the madhhab. But al-Khazzazi refused to agree and did not leave the city, but remained in his house until he died, patient in his religion, clinging to the strength of his certainty.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry for Abu 'Abd Allah al-Khazzazi, a reciter (muqri') and scholar of Nishapur. It opens with a citation from al-Farsi, reporting that al-Khazzazi died in Ramadan 447 AH, and that his funeral prayer was led by Abu 'Uthman. He traveled to al-Kashmihani specifically to hear the 'Sahih' of al-Sabuni, and he was so reliable that people depended on his hearing and his manuscript copy of the work. His piety is highlighted: he would spend nights in recitation, prayer, and weeping, and his supplications were believed to be answered. A second account from al-Tabasi describes his conduct during the trial (mihnah) under the vizier al-Kunduri, when the Ash'ari or Shafi'i scholars in Nishapur were pressured to renounce their madhhab. Some scholars left the city or complied with the demand to disavow their school, but al-Khazzazi refused both options: he neither left nor agreed to disavow. Instead, he remained in his house until his death, steadfast in his religion, relying on the strength of his certainty. The passage emphasizes his steadfastness and piety.

Key Terms

محنة
مذهب
صابر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Khazzazi's refusal to leave or renounce was stubbornness or extremism.
Correction: The passage frames his action as patient perseverance and strength of certainty, not obstinacy.

Checkpoint
How did al-Khazzazi respond to the persecution under al-Kunduri?
A) He left Nishapur
B) He agreed to renounce his madhhab
C) He stayed in his house and refused to renounce
D) He argued with the authorities

Key Takeaway
Al-Khazzazi's steadfastness during the mihnah illustrates that true certainty (yaqin) enables patient endurance without compromise or flight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when your beliefs were tested by social pressure or authority. Did you, like some of those scholars, feel the urge to flee or outwardly conform? What does your response reveal about the strength of your certainty (yaqin)?
?
Al-Khazzazi remained in his house, neither fleeing nor compromising. What 'house' – what sphere of life – might you need to remain steadfast in right now, even if it means facing isolation or hardship? What fears hold you back from that patience?
?
If someone were to ask you, 'Why do you hold on to your faith despite pressure?' would your answer be rooted in deep certainty or mere habit? What one change can you make this week to deepen your yaqin so that it becomes a fortress for you?

Biography of Abu al-Fadl ibn 'Amrus al-Baghdadi al-Maliki
Two reports on the life of Ibn 'Amrus, focusing on his scholarly credentials and authority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the life and scholarly authority of Ibn 'Amrus as a leading Maliki jurist in Baghdad.

Core Idea
A scholar's trustworthiness and knowledge made him the supreme fatwa authority.

Ibn 'Asakir presents the biography of Abu al-Fadl ibn 'Amrus through two chains of transmission, highlighting his qualifications as a Maliki jurist, Qur'an memorizer, and the ultimate reference for fatwa in Baghdad. This lesson traces his scholarly journey and the recognition he received from peers and judges.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of Ibn 'Amrus's life.
Notice the two separate reports from different narrators.
2
Identify key attributes
Note the qualities mentioned: jurist, hafiz of Quran, teacher, trustworthy, religious.
3
Recognize his status
The passage states that 'to him ended the giving of fatwa in fiqh according to Malik in Baghdad' and that the chief qadi accepted his testimony.
This indicates he was the highest Maliki authority in the city.
4
Note biographical details
Find his birth year (Rajab 372 AH), death year (Muharram 452 AH), and his residence (Bab al-Sham).
5
Compare the two reports
The second report from al-Shirazi adds that he was a scholar of usul (principles) and righteous, confirming the same death year.

أخبرنا الشريف أبو القسم على بن إبراهيم بن العباس العلوى والشيخ أبو الحسن على بن أحمد بن منصور الغساني وأبو منصور بن خيرون قالوا قال لنا أبو بكر أحمد بن على بن ثابت الخطيب الحافظ : محمد بن عبيد الله أحمد بن محمد بن عمروس أبو الفضل البزاز كان أحد الفقهاء على مذهب مالك وكان أيضا من حفاظ القرآن ومدرسيه ، سمع أبا القسم ابن حبابة وأبا حفص بن شاهين وأبا طاهر المخلص وأبا القسم بن الصيدلاني كتبت عنه وكان ديناً ثقة مستورا وإليه انتهت الفتوى في الـفـقـه علـى مالك ببغداد ، وقبل قاضى القضاء أبو عبد الله الدامغانی شهادته ، وكان يسكن بباب الشام سألت أبا الفضل عن مولده فقال في رجب من سنة أثنتين وسبعين وثلاثمائة ، وبلغنا ونحن بدمشق أنه مات في أول المحرم سنة أثنتين وخمسين وأربعمائة . من أخبرنا الشيخ أبو القسم إسماعيل بن أحمد بن عمر بن السمرقندی قال ثنا الشيخ الإمام أبو إسحق إبراهيم بن على بن يوسف الشيرازي لفظا قال ومنهم أبو الفضل بن عمروس البغدادي المالكي وكان فقيهاً أصولياً صالحاً مات سنة أثنتين وخمسين وأربعمائة
Narrated to us al-Sharif Abu al-Qasim 'Ali ibn Ibrahim ibn al-'Abbas al-'Alawi, and Shaykh Abu al-Hasan 'Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Mansur al-Ghassani, and Abu Mansur ibn Khayrun, they said: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn 'Ali ibn Thabit al-Khatib al-Hafiz said to us: Muhammad ibn 'Ubayd Allah Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn 'Amrus, Abu al-Fadl al-Bazzaz, was one of the jurists of the Maliki school, and also one of the memorizers and teachers of the Qur'an. He heard from Abu al-Qasim ibn Hababa, Abu Hafs ibn Shahin, Abu Tahir al-Mukhlis, and Abu al-Qasim ibn al-Saydalani. I wrote from him, and he was religious, trustworthy, and upright. To him ended the giving of fatwa in fiqh according to Malik in Baghdad. The chief qadi Abu 'Abd Allah al-Damaghani accepted his testimony. He resided at Bab al-Sham. I asked Abu al-Fadl about his birth, and he said: in Rajab of the year 372. And it reached us while we were in Damascus that he died at the beginning of Muharram of the year 452. From: narrated to us Shaykh Abu al-Qasim Isma'il ibn Ahmad ibn 'Umar ibn al-Samarqandi, he said: narrated to us Shaykh Imam Abu Ishaq Ibrahim ibn 'Ali ibn Yusuf al-Shirazi orally, he said: Among them is Abu al-Fadl ibn 'Amrus al-Baghdadi al-Maliki, and he was a jurist, a scholar of usul, and righteous; he died in the year 452.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents two reports on the biography of Abu al-Fadl ibn 'Amrus, a Maliki jurist in Baghdad. The first, transmitted through al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, describes his qualifications: he was a jurist of the Maliki school, a memorizer (hafiz) of the Qur'an, and a teacher of Qur'anic recitation. He studied under several shaykhs, including Abu al-Qasim ibn Hababa and others. Al-Khatib himself wrote from him and attests to his religiosity, trustworthiness, and integrity. The high point of his career: he was the ultimate authority for fatwa in Maliki fiqh in Baghdad, and the chief qadi al-Damaghani accepted his legal testimony, indicating his reliability. He lived at Bab al-Sham in Baghdad. When asked about his birth, he said Rajab 372 AH. News reached the narrator in Damascus that he died at the beginning of Muharram 452 AH. The second report, from al-Shirazi via al-Samarqandi, confirms his status as a jurist, a scholar of usul (principles), and a righteous person, and gives the same death year (452 AH). The passage establishes Ibn 'Amrus as the leading Maliki authority in Baghdad, a model of scholarship and piety.

Key Takeaway
Ibn 'Amrus was the preeminent Maliki scholar in Baghdad, recognized for his knowledge, trustworthiness, and piety, and served as the ultimate authority for fatwa.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn 'Amrus was described as 'religious, trustworthy, and upright' (dīn, thiqah, mastūr). If those who know you best were to describe your character with three words, what would they be? Would you be satisfied if they matched his? What one thing could you start doing today to move closer to that description?
?
The chief qadi accepted his testimony—meaning Ibn 'Amrus's word carried weight in the most serious of matters. In your own life, whose word do you trust completely? More importantly, are you that kind of person for anyone? If not, what is the first step to becoming someone whose testimony is trusted in matters of truth?
?
He was both a merchant (bazzaz) and a scholar who reached the highest level of fatwa authority. Do you separate your worldly work from your religious learning? How can you combine them, as he did, so that your daily profession does not hinder but rather supports your pursuit and application of knowledge?

Al-Khabbazi's Steadfastness in Persecution
Ibn Asakir reports a story of al-Khabbazi's steadfastness during persecution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Khabbazi's response to persecution exemplifies patience and certainty in the Ash'ari tradition.

Core Idea
True steadfastness is patient endurance, not passive surrender.

This brief but powerful anecdote from Ibn Asakir captures a moment of trial for the Ash'ari community in Nishapur. It presents three distinct responses to pressure: flight, disavowal, and patient refusal. Al-Khabbazi's choice to stay and remain silent in his conviction offers a deep lesson in spiritual resilience.

Guided Path
1
Read the story and translation
Read the Arabic passage once silently, then read the English translation. Notice the key verbs describing each person's action.
2
Identify the three responses
The passage lists three types of behavior from the tested companions. List them: leaving the city, agreeing to disavow, and refusing both. Which one does al-Khabbazi choose?
3
Reflect on al-Khabbazi's posture
Al-Khabbazi does not flee nor disavow. Instead he stays home, patient, clinging to certainty. Ask yourself: what made this possible?
The text emphasizes 'strength of his certainty' (quwwat yaqīnihi).
4
Connect to the broader lesson
Consider how this story illustrates the virtue of ṣabr (patience) in times of tribulation, especially for scholars. How might this encourage a believer today?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سمعت الشيخ أبا المحاسن عبد الرزاق بن محمد الطبسي بنيسابور، يحكى عن بعض مشايخه أنه لما امتحن أصحابنا بنيسابور في أيام الكندرى، كان فيهم من خرج عن البلد، وفيهم من أجاب إلى التبرى من المذهب وأن الخبازى امتنع من الإجابة ولم يخرج من البلد ولازم بيته إلى أن مات صابراً على دينه معتصماً بقوة يقينه
I heard the Shaykh Abu al-Mahasin Abd al-Razzaq ibn Muhammad al-Tabasi in Nishapur, relating from some of his shaykhs that when our companions were tested in Nishapur in the days of al-Kunduri, among them were those who left the city, and among them were those who responded to disavowal of the school, and that al-Khabbazi refused to respond and did not leave the city, and he remained in his house until he died, patient upon his religion, clinging to the strength of his certainty.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a biographical anecdote transmitted by Ibn Asakir through a chain. It describes a historical trial (imtiḥān) in Nishapur during the vizierate of al-Kunduri, where Ash'ari scholars were pressured to disavow their school. Some fled, some acquiesced, but al-Khabbazi refused to renounce his school or flee; he stayed in his home and died patiently, relying on the strength of his yaqīn (certainty). The story illustrates steadfastness in the face of persecution.
