The Scholar's Teaching, Prestige, and Major Work
Ibn Asakir describes the scholar's teaching, students, and composition of Nihayat al-Matlab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the life and contributions of a great Shafi'i scholar as depicted by Ibn Asakir, focusing on his teaching impact, high status, and the composition of his magnum opus, Nihayat al-Matlab.

Core Idea
A scholar's dedication to teaching and his magnum opus set a new standard in Islamic scholarship.

This passage from Ibn Asakir's work introduces us to a towering figure in Islamic scholarship. It details how his teaching produced a generation of scholars, his high status with rulers, and the composition of his masterpiece, Nihayat al-Matlab. We see a model of scholarly excellence that combined teaching, authorship, and influence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the complete Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overall sense of the scholar's profile. Note the sequence: teaching, status, works, journey, major book.
2
Identify the scholar's teaching impact
Notice how the passage highlights that many students became teachers themselves. This shows the scholar's effectiveness in producing future scholars.
The phrase 'تخرج به جماعة من الأئمة' indicates direct student output.
3
Analyze his relationship with authorities
Observe the details about acceptance by the sultan and viziers. The scholar's word in fatwa was final, and he was given leadership over the group and endowments.
This reflects the integration of scholarship and political power.
4
Understand his major work
Focus on the description of 'Nihayat al-Matlab'. Note the process of composition, the completion session, and the praise it received. The passage claims no such work existed before.
5
Reflect on the overall significance
Consider the final sentence: anyone who compares his method to earlier scholars will acknowledge his lofty station. This sums up the passage's claim of his unparalleled contribution.

الطلبة وتخرج به جماعة من الأئمة والفحول وأولاد الصدور، حتى بلغوا محل التدريس في زمانه وانتظم بإقباله على العلم ومواظبته على التدريس والمناظرة والمباحثة أسباب ومحافل ومجامع وإمعان في طلب العلم وسوق نافقة لأهله لم يعهد قبله وأتصل به ما يليق بمنصبه من القبول عند السلطان والوزير والأركان ووفور الحشمة عندهم بحيث لا يذكر غيره فكان المخاطب والمشار إليه والمقبول من قبله والمهجور من هجره والمصدر في المجالس من ينتمى إلى خدمته والمنظور إليه من يغترف في الأصول والفروع من طريقته، واتفق منه تصانيف برسم الحضرة النظامية مثل النظامي والغياثي وانفاذها إلى الحضرة ووقوعها موقع القبول ومقابلتها بما يليق بها من الشكر والرضى، والخلع الفائقة والمراكب المثمنة والهدايا والمرسومات وكذلك إلى أن قلد زعامة الأصحاب ورياسة الطائفة وفوض إليه أمور الأوقاف وصارت حشمته وزر العلماء والأئمة والقضاة وقوله في الفتوى مرجع العظماء والأكابر والولاة، واتفقت له نهضة في أعلى ما كان من أيامه إلى أصبهان لسبب مخالفة بعض الأصحاب فلقى بها من المجلس النظامي ما كان من اللائق بمنصبه من الاستبشار والاعزاز والاكرام بأنواع المبر وأجيب بما كان فوق مطلوبه وعاد مكرماً إلى نيسابور، وصار أكثر عنايته مصروفاً إلى تصنيف المذهب الكبير المسمى ( نهاية المطلب في دراية المذهب ) حتى حرره وأملاه وأتى فيه من البحث والتقدير والسبك والتنقير والتدقيق والتحقيق بما شفى الغليل وأوضح السبيل ونبه على قدره ومحله في علم الشريعة، ودرس للخواص من التلامذه وفرغ منه ومن إتمامه فعقد مجلساً لتتمة الكتاب حضره الأئمة والكبار وختم الكتاب على رسم الإملاء والاستملاء، وتبجح الجماعة بذلك ودعوا له واثنوا عليه وكان من المعتدين باتمام ذلك الشاكرين الله عليه فما صنف في الإسلام قبله مثله ولا اتفق لأحد ما اتفق له، ومن قاس طريقته بطريقة المتقدمين في الأصول والفروع وأنصف أقر بعلو منصبه ووفور تعبه ونصبه، في الدين وكثرة سهره في استنباط الغوامض وتحقيق المسائل وترتيب الدلائل
The students and a group of imams, eminent scholars, and sons of dignitaries graduated under him, until they reached the level of teaching in his time. His attention to knowledge, his persistence in teaching, debating, and research organized means, assemblies, gatherings, and diligence in seeking knowledge—a thriving market for its people unknown before him. There was attached to him what befits his position of acceptance by the sultan, the vizier, and the pillars, and abundant respect from them such that no one else was mentioned. So he was the addressed, the pointed out, the accepted from him, the forsaken was the one he forsook, the reference in councils, those who attach themselves to his service, and the one looked to who drinks from his method in usul and furu'. Works were produced by him for the Nizamiyya court like al-Nizami and al-Ghiyathi, and their dispatch to the court, their acceptance, and their being met with appropriate gratitude and satisfaction, with excellent robes of honor, costly mounts, gifts, and grants. Similarly, he was entrusted with the leadership of the companions and the presidency of the group. The affairs of the endowments were delegated to him, and his dignity became the adornment of scholars, imams, and judges, and his word in fatwa was the reference of the great, the eminent, and the governors. He undertook a journey at the height of his days to Isfahan due to a conflict with some companions. He met there from the Nizamiyya council what was befitting his position of joy, honor, and respect with various kinds of kindness. He was given more than he sought and returned honored to Nishapur. His attention became mostly directed to composing the great work on the school called 'Nihayat al-Matlab fi Dirayat al-Madhhab' until he revised it and dictated it, and brought in it of research, estimation, refinement, investigation, verification, and realization what quenched the thirst, clarified the path, and indicated his rank and place in the science of Sharia. He taught the elite of students and finished it. Upon its completion, he held a session for the completion of the book attended by imams and the great, and he concluded the book in the manner of dictation and transcription. The group took delight in that, prayed for him, and praised him. He was among those who completed it, thanking God for it. No work like it was composed in Islam before him, nor was what happened for him attained by anyone. Whoever compares his method to the method of the earlier scholars in usul and furu' and is fair will acknowledge the loftiness of his station, the abundance of his toil and exertion in religion, and his frequent sleeplessness in deducing subtle points, verifying issues, and arranging proofs.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a biographical description of a great scholar (likely Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni). It begins by noting that many students, including imams and dignitaries, graduated under him. His dedication to teaching and research created an unprecedented scholarly environment. The text then details his high status with the ruling authorities—he was the only one consulted and honored. He authored works for the Nizamiyya court, such as 'al-Nizami' and 'al-Ghiyathi', which were received with gratitude and gifts. He was given leadership over the companions and endowments. A journey to Isfahan due to a conflict with some companions resulted in great honor, and he returned to Nishapur. His main focus became the composition of his magnum opus, 'Nihayat al-Matlab fi Dirayat al-Madhhab', a comprehensive work on Shafi'i jurisprudence. He completed it with a special session attended by imams. The passage concludes by asserting the unmatched excellence of his work and his tireless effort in deducing nuanced points and arranging proofs.

Al-Bakharzi's Praise Through Comparisons
Al-Bakharzi's eulogy comparing the scholar to great imams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Bakharzi used comparative praise to elevate the scholar's stature among the great imams.

Core Idea
A scholar's excellence is measured by the company he keeps in praise.

This passage from Ibn Asakir's work quotes al-Bakharzi's eloquent eulogy. It is not a dry list of achievements but a rhetorical masterpiece that compares the scholar to the greatest imams. Understanding this helps us appreciate how medieval biographers used language to convey spiritual and intellectual rank.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the rhythmic prose and imagery sink in.
2
Identify the comparisons
Find each named imam and the quality for which the scholar is compared to them: jurisprudence (al-Shafi'i), literature (al-Asma'i), preaching (al-Hasan al-Basri).
Notice the pattern: 'So jurisprudence is the jurisprudence of al-Shafi'i, and literature is the literature of al-Asma'i...'
3
Examine the hyperbole
Look for phrases that amplify the scholar's stature: 'the imam of every imam', 'the victor over every lion'. Understand this as rhetorical convention, not literal claim.
Hyperbolic praise was common in medieval Arabic eulogies.
4
Reflect on the obscure final sentence
Consider the meaning of the last line about 'the school of hadith would have become a hadith'. This likely means the scholar's role preserved the tradition.
The exact phrasing is debated; the key is his indispensable role.
5
Consider the purpose
Ask yourself: why does al-Bakharzi use these comparisons? To establish the scholar's supreme rank and to inspire reverence in the reader.
This praise serves as a biographical monument within the larger work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقد قرأت فصلاً ذكره علي بن الحسن بن أبي الطيب الباخرزي في كتاب ( دمية القصر ) مشتملا على حاله وهو فقد كان في عصر الشباب غير مستكمل ما عهدناه عليه من اتساق الأسباب وهو أن قال فتى الفتيان ومن اتجب به الفتيان ولم يخرج مثله المفتيان عنيت النعمان بن ثابت ومحمد بن إدريس، فالفقه فقه الشافعي والأدب أدب الأصمعي، وحسن بصره بالوعظ للحسن البصري وكيف ما هو فهو إمام كل إمام والمستعلى بهمته على كل همام والفائز بالظفر على أرغام كل ضرغام إذا تصدر للفقه فالمزني من مزنته قطره، وإذا تكلم فالأشعري من وفرته شعره وإذا خطب ألجم الفصحاء بغى شقاشقه الهادرة ولثم البلغاء بالصمت حقائقه البادرة ولولا سده مكان أبيه بسده الذي أفرغ على قطرة قطر تأبيه لأصبح مذهب الحديث حديثًا ولم يجد المستغيث منهم مغيثاً
And I read a passage mentioned by Ali ibn al-Hasan ibn Abi al-Tayyib al-Bakharzi in the book 'Damyat al-Qasr' containing his state, and he was in the time of youth not having completed what we have known him of the coherence of causes. He said: 'The youth of youths and one by whom youths are chosen, and the two imams (Abu Hanifa and Shafi'i) did not produce his like—I mean al-Nu'man ibn Thabit and Muhammad ibn Idris. So jurisprudence is the jurisprudence of al-Shafi'i, and literature is the literature of al-Asma'i, and his insight into preaching is that of al-Hasan al-Basri. And however he is, he is the imam of every imam, the one who rises with his ambition over every ambitious one, the victor with triumph over the spite of every lion. When he sits for jurisprudence, al-Muzani is from his raincloud a drop; when he speaks, al-Ash'ari is from his abundance a hair; when he delivers a sermon, he bridles the eloquent with the roar of his overflowing [speech] and silences the eloquent with the silence of his manifest truths. Were it not for his filling the place of his father with his filling that poured upon the drop of his [؟] reluctance, the school of hadith would have become a hadith, and the seeker of help would not have found a helper among them.'
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a quotation from al-Bakharzi's biographical work 'Damyat al-Qasr' praising the scholar. It uses a series of hyperbolic comparisons to establish his greatness: he is the best of youths, surpassing even the two great imams Abu Hanifa and al-Shafi'i. His jurisprudence is that of al-Shafi'i, his literature that of al-Asma'i, his preaching like al-Hasan al-Basri. He is the imam of all imams. When he teaches jurisprudence, even the great al-Muzani is but a drop from his cloud; when he speaks, al-Ash'ari is but a hair from his abundance. His sermons overpower eloquent speakers. The final sentence is obscure but suggests that without his filling his father's place, the school of hadith would have lost its way.

الحسن البصري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage claims the scholar is literally superior to the Prophet (peace be upon him).
Correction: The comparisons are limited to other human scholars and imams; such hyperbolic praise is a literary convention and does not extend to prophethood.

Key Takeaway
Al-Bakharzi's eulogy uses comparative rhetoric to place the scholar among the greatest imams, highlighting his excellence in jurisprudence, literature, and preaching.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
If someone were to write a eulogy for your life right now, what comparisons would they honestly make? What would those comparisons reveal about what you truly value?

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir

The Scholar's Teaching Sessions and Eloquence
Ibn Asakir enumerates the scholar's teaching sessions and expresses regret.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the diverse teaching activities of the scholar as observed by Ibn Asakir.

Core Idea
The scholar's teaching encompassed eloquence, debate, public preaching, and high-level discussion.

Ibn Asakir describes firsthand the scholar's remarkable teaching sessions. From eloquent discourses to deep debates and emotional preaching, the scholar excelled in every format. This passage highlights the range and depth of his scholarly activities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to understand the flow.
Notice the repetition of 'كم من' which introduces four types of sessions.
2
Identify the four types of sessions
The passage lists four distinct types of scholarly gatherings. Find each one and note their characteristics.
They range from eloquent speeches to public preaching and high-level discussions.
3
Reflect on the emotional and intellectual mix
The preaching sessions combined jurisprudential gems, theological principles, and emotional elements like weeping and joy.
This shows the scholar's ability to engage both mind and heart.
4
Consider the narrator's regret
Ibn Asakir regrets not fully appreciating the scholar during his lifetime. Why might this be a common feeling?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو الحسن هذا وهو وحق الحق فوق ما ذكره وأعلى مما وصفه فكم من فصل مشتمل على العبارات الفصيحة العالية والنكت البديعة النادرة في المحافل منه سمعناه، وكم من مسائل في النظر شهدناه ورأينا منه إفحام الخصوم وعهدناه، وكم من مجلس في التذكير للعوام مسلسل المسائل مشحون بالنكت المستنبطة من مسائل الفقه مشتملة على حقائق الأصول مبكية في التحذير مفرحة في التبشير مختومة بالدعوات وفنون المناجاة حضرناه، وكم من مجمع للتدريس حاو للكبار من الأئمة ولقاء المسائل عليهم والمباحثة في غورها رأيناه، وحصلنا بعض ما أمكننا منه وعلقناه، ولم نقدر ما كنا فيه من نضرة أيامه وزرهرة شهوره وأعوامه حق قدره، ولم نشكر الله تعالى عليه حق شكره حتى فقدناه وسلبناه، وسمعته في أثناء كلام يقول أنا لا أنام لا آكل عادة وإنما أنام إذا غلبني النوم ليلاً كان أو نهارا واكل إذا اشتهيت الطعام أي وقت كان، كان لذته ولهوه وتنزهه في مذاكرة العلم وطلب من أي نوع كان، ولقد سمعت الشيخ أبا الحسن علي بن فضال بن علي المجاشعي النحوي القادم علينا سنة تسع وستين وأربعمائة يقول: وقد قبله الإمام فخر الإسلام وقابله بالاكرام وأخذ في قراءة النحو عليه والتلمذة
Abu al-Hasan said: This—and by the truth of truth, he is above what he mentioned and higher than what he described. How many sections containing eloquent high expressions and beautiful rare gems in gatherings did we hear from him? How many issues in discussion did we witness? And we saw from him the silencing of opponents and we have known that. How many sessions for reminding the common people, serial in issues, filled with gems derived from jurisprudential issues, containing realities of usul, making one weep in warning, making one joyful in glad tidings, concluded with supplications and various forms of intimate prayer—did we attend? How many teaching gatherings containing the great imams and the presentation of issues to them and discussion in their depths did we see? We obtained some of what we could from him and wrote it down. We did not fully appreciate the freshness of his days and the bloom of his months and years as they deserved, nor did we thank God for him as He deserved until we lost him and were deprived of him. I heard him during speech saying: 'I do not sleep, I do not eat habitually; I only sleep when sleep overcomes me, night or day, and I eat when I desire food, at any time.' His pleasure, amusement, and relaxation was in discussing knowledge and seeking it of any kind. And I heard Shaykh Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Faddal ibn Ali al-Mujashi'i al-Nahwi, who came to us in the year 469 AH, saying: 'And Imam Fakhr al-Islam accepted him and received him with honor, and he began reading grammar with him and studying.'
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is the narrator's (likely Ibn Asakir's) personal commentary after quoting al-Bakharzi. He affirms that the scholar's actual excellence surpasses even that hyperbolic description. He then lists the types of scholarly sessions he witnessed: eloquent discourses, public preaching sessions that combined jurisprudence and usul with emotional impact, and high-level teaching gatherings with leading imams. The narrator regrets not fully appreciating the scholar's lifetime. He then recalls a personal remark from the scholar about his habits: he does not sleep or eat by routine, but only sleeps when overcome and eats when hungry, regardless of time. His only delight was discussing and seeking knowledge. The passage ends with a report from a grammarian who studied with the scholar, confirming his acceptance and honor.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir witnessed the scholar's multifaceted teaching, ranging from eloquent public addresses to deep scholarly debates, and regretted not cherishing it enough.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The narrator regrets not thanking Allah properly for the scholar until he lost him. Is there a teacher or scholar you have not thanked adequately? Write down one specific way you will express gratitude to them this week.

The Scholar's Personal Habits and Devotion to Knowledge
Ibn Asakir recounts the scholar's personal habits and a grammarian's testimony.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholar's personal discipline and single-minded devotion to knowledge.

Core Idea
The scholar lived without routine, sleeping and eating only when necessary; his only delight was knowledge.

After describing the scholar's teaching, Ibn Asakir shares personal observations of his ascetic habits. The scholar's life was unbound by convention, and his ultimate joy was discussing and seeking knowledge. A grammarian's account confirms the scholar's generous reception of students.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the scholar's own words about his habits.
Note the contrast with habitual routines.
2
Understand the scholar's statement
The scholar says he does not sleep or eat habitually; he only sleeps when overcome and eats when hungry, at any time.
This shows his asceticism and flexibility.
3
Reflect on his delight in knowledge
His pleasure, amusement, and relaxation (لذته ولهوه وتنزهه) were in discussing and seeking knowledge of any kind.
This highlights his total dedication.
4
Consider the grammarian's testimony
Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Mujashi'i reports that the scholar accepted him with honor and taught him grammar.
This confirms his generosity as a teacher.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو الحسن هذا وهو وحق الحق فوق ما ذكره وأعلى مما وصفه فكم من فصل مشتمل على العبارات الفصيحة العالية والنكت البديعة النادرة في المحافل منه سمعناه، وكم من مسائل في النظر شهدناه ورأينا منه إفحام الخصوم وعهدناه، وكم من مجلس في التذكير للعوام مسلسل المسائل مشحون بالنكت المستنبطة من مسائل الفقه مشتملة على حقائق الأصول مبكية في التحذير مفرحة في التبشير مختومة بالدعوات وفنون المناجاة حضرناه، وكم من مجمع للتدريس حاو للكبار من الأئمة ولقاء المسائل عليهم والمباحثة في غورها رأيناه، وحصلنا بعض ما أمكننا منه وعلقناه، ولم نقدر ما كنا فيه من نضرة أيامه وزرهرة شهوره وأعوامه حق قدره، ولم نشكر الله تعالى عليه حق شكره حتى فقدناه وسلبناه، وسمعته في أثناء كلام يقول أنا لا أنام لا آكل عادة وإنما أنام إذا غلبني النوم ليلاً كان أو نهارا واكل إذا اشتهيت الطعام أي وقت كان، كان لذته ولهوه وتنزهه في مذاكرة العلم وطلب من أي نوع كان، ولقد سمعت الشيخ أبا الحسن علي بن فضال بن علي المجاشعي النحوي القادم علينا سنة تسع وستين وأربعمائة يقول: وقد قبله الإمام فخر الإسلام وقابله بالاكرام وأخذ في قراءة النحو عليه والتلمذة
Abu al-Hasan said: This—and by the truth of truth, he is above what he mentioned and higher than what he described. How many sections containing eloquent high expressions and beautiful rare gems in gatherings did we hear from him? How many issues in discussion did we witness? And we saw from him the silencing of opponents and we have known that. How many sessions for reminding the common people, serial in issues, filled with gems derived from jurisprudential issues, containing realities of usul, making one weep in warning, making one joyful in glad tidings, concluded with supplications and various forms of intimate prayer—did we attend? How many teaching gatherings containing the great imams and the presentation of issues to them and discussion in their depths did we see? We obtained some of what we could from him and wrote it down. We did not fully appreciate the freshness of his days and the bloom of his months and years as they deserved, nor did we thank God for him as He deserved until we lost him and were deprived of him. I heard him during speech saying: 'I do not sleep, I do not eat habitually; I only sleep when sleep overcomes me, night or day, and I eat when I desire food, at any time.' His pleasure, amusement, and relaxation was in discussing knowledge and seeking it of any kind. And I heard Shaykh Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Faddal ibn Ali al-Mujashi'i al-Nahwi, who came to us in the year 469 AH, saying: 'And Imam Fakhr al-Islam accepted him and received him with honor, and he began reading grammar with him and studying.'
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is the narrator's (likely Ibn Asakir's) personal commentary after quoting al-Bakharzi. He affirms that the scholar's actual excellence surpasses even that hyperbolic description. He then lists the types of scholarly sessions he witnessed: eloquent discourses, public preaching sessions that combined jurisprudence and usul with emotional impact, and high-level teaching gatherings with leading imams. The narrator regrets not fully appreciating the scholar's lifetime. He then recalls a personal remark from the scholar about his habits: he does not sleep or eat by routine, but only sleeps when overcome and eats when hungry, regardless of time. His only delight was discussing and seeking knowledge. The passage ends with a report from a grammarian who studied with the scholar, confirming his acceptance and honor.

فخر الإسلام

Correction: This scholar slept only when sleep overcame him and ate only when hungry, showing that dedication to knowledge can transcend routine.

Al-Bakhrizi's Poetic Praise of the Imam
Al-Bakhrizi's poetic eulogy for the Imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars used poetic hyperbole to praise an Imam and what comparisons reveal about the Imam's stature across multiple disciplines.

Core Idea
Poetic praise elevates the Imam above all other imams in every field.

This passage from al-Bakhrizi's Damiyat al-Qasr offers a lavish poetic tribute to the subject of Ibn 'Asakir's biography. The panegyric uses exaggerated comparisons to establish the Imam's supremacy in fiqh, adab, wa'z, and oratory. We'll explore how such praise functioned in classical Islamic scholarship and what it reveals about the author's reverence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then review the translation. Let the poetic rhythm and hyperbolic style sink in before you interpret.
Notice the series of comparisons that structure the praise.
2
Identify the figures compared
List the earlier scholars to whom the Imam is compared: Abu Hanifa, al-Shafi'i, al-Asma'i, al-Hasan al-Basri, al-Muzani, al-Ash'ari. Reflect on the significance of each comparison.
These are major figures in fiqh, adab, wa'z, and kalam.
3
Understand the rhetorical purpose
Ask yourself whether the praise is meant literally or as hyperbole. The common misunderstanding warns against taking it as a literal claim of superiority over the earlier imams.
Such panegyric was a recognized genre with its own conventions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقد قرأت فصلاً ذكره على بن الحسن بن أبي الطيب الباخرزي في كتاب ) دمية القصر ) مشتملا على حاله وهو فقد كان في عصر الشباب غير مستكمل ما عهدناه عليه من اتساق الأسباب وهو أن قال فتى الفتيان ومن اتجب به الفتيان ولم يخرج مثله المفتيان عنيت النعمان بن ثابت . ومحمد بن أدريس ، فالفقه فقه الشافعي والأدب أدب الأصمعي ، وحسن بصره بالوعظ للحسن البصرى وكيف ما هو فهو إمام كل إمام والمستعلى بهمته على كل همام والفائز بالظفر على أرغام كل ضرغام إذا تصدر للفقه فالمزني من مزنته قطره ، وإذا تكلم فالاشعرى من وفرته شعره وإذا خطب ألجم الفصحاء بالغى شقاشقه الهادرة ولثم البلغاء بالصمت حقائقه البادرة ولولا سده مكان أبيه بسده الذى أفرغ على قطرة قطر تأبيه لأصبح مذهب الحديث حديثًا ولم يجد المستغيث منهم مغيثاً.
And I have read a section mentioned by Ali ibn al-Hasan ibn Abi al-Tayyib al-Bakhrizi in the book (Damiyat al-Qasr) containing his condition, and he was in his youth not having completed what we had witnessed of his harmonious circumstances, and he said: The youth of youths and the one by whom youths are chosen, and the two muftis did not produce the like of him—I mean al-Nu'man ibn Thabit and Muhammad ibn Idris. So jurisprudence is the jurisprudence of al-Shafi'i, and literature is the literature of al-Asma'i, and his insight in preaching is that of al-Hasan al-Basri. However he is, he is the imam of every imam, the one exalted by his ambition over every determined one, the one victorious over the noses of every lion. When he presides for jurisprudence, al-Muzani is from his cloud a drop; when he speaks, al-Ash'ari is from his abundance a hair; when he delivers a sermon, he silences the eloquent with the roar of his thundering eloquence and makes the eloquent kiss the dust of his brilliant truths. Were it not for his taking the place of his father in a station that poured over a drop that his father refused, the school of hadith would have become a novelty, and the seeker of help would have found no helper.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a quoted eulogy from al-Bakhrizi's work 'Damiyat al-Qasr', praising an unnamed Imam (the subject of the biography). The author begins by noting he read a section describing the Imam's state in his youth, before he had fully matured. The quote then praises the Imam as the foremost youth, chosen above others, and claims that even the two great muftis—Abu Hanifa (al-Nu'man ibn Thabit) and al-Shafi'i (Muhammad ibn Idris)—did not produce someone like him. This is hyperbolic praise. The quote continues by attributing to him the best of various disciplines: his fiqh is that of al-Shafi'i, his adab that of al-Asma'i, his wa'z (preaching) that of al-Hasan al-Basri. He is called the imam of every imam, exalted by his ambition. Specific comparisons are made: when he teaches fiqh, al-Muzani (a prominent Shafi'i) is but a drop from his cloud; when he speaks, al-Ash'ari (the theologian) is but a hair from his abundance. His sermons silence eloquent speakers with their force. The final line warns that were it not for his occupying his father's position, the 'madhhab of hadith' would have become something novel and the helpless would have no helper—implying his role in preserving orthodoxy.

النعمان بن ثابت
محمد بن إدريس
المزني
الاشعري
الحسن البصري
الأصمعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Imam is literally superior to Abu Hanifa, al-Shafi'i, al-Ash'ari, etc. in their respective fields.
Correction: The praise is hyperbolic, intended to elevate his status in his own time, not to claim literal superiority over the earlier imams in all respects.

Key Takeaway
This poetic praise uses hyperbole to assert the Imam's comprehensive excellence and his role as a guardian of orthodoxy.

?

?
The Imam is likened to giants of fiqh, adab, and wa'z. Which of these three areas is most lacking in your life, and what concrete step will you take this week to nurture it?

The Author's Testimony of the Imam's Virtues
Ibn Asakir enumerates his firsthand experiences of the Imam's sessions and expresses regret at his loss.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir personally witnessed and described the multifaceted virtues of the Imam through various teaching and preaching sessions.

Core Idea
Ibn Asakir's firsthand account brings the Imam's virtues to life beyond secondhand praise.

In the previous passage, al-Bakhrizi offered high praise of the Imam. Now, Ibn Asakir adds his own testimony, drawing on what he personally witnessed. He describes the Imam's eloquent assemblies, debates with opponents, preaching sessions for the common people, and teaching gatherings with senior scholars. This lesson explores these firsthand observations and the author's sense of loss.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the rhythm of the author's descriptions sink in.
Pay attention to the repetition of 'كم من' – it signals a list of experiences.
2
Identify the types of sessions
The passage mentions several kinds of gatherings: eloquent assemblies, speculative debates, reminding sessions for the common people, and teaching gatherings for senior imams. Find each one in the text.
3
Notice the emotional tone and regret
The passage begins with praise but ends with a poignant regret at not appreciating the Imam's blessings until after his death. Observe the shift in tone.
The author says he 'did not appreciate its due measure' and thanks Allah late.
4
Consider the significance of firsthand testimony
Why does Ibn Asakir include his own witness here? How does it strengthen the biography? Think about the trustworthiness of personal experience in historical writing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو الحسن هذا وهو وحق الحق فوق ما ذكره وأعلى مما وصفه فكم من فصل مشتمل على العبارات الفصيحة العالية والنكت البديعة النادرة فى المحافل منه سمعناه ، وكم من مسائل في النظر شهدناه ورأينا منه إفحام الخصوم وعهدناه ، وكم من مجلس فى التذكير للعوام مسلسل المسائل مشحون بالنكت المستنبطة من مسائل الفقه مشتملة على حقائق الأصول مبكية في التحذير مفرحة في التبشير مختومة بالدعوات وفنون المناجاة حضرناه ، وكم من مجمع للتدريس حاو للكبار من الأئمة ولقاء المسائل عليهم والمباحثة في غورها رأيناه ، وحصلنا بعض ما أمكننا منه وعلقناه ، ولم نقدر ما كنا فيه من نضرة أيامه وزرهرة شهوره وأعوامه حق قدره ، ولم نشكر الله تعالى عليه حق شكره حتى فقدناه وسلبناه.
Abu al-Hasan said: This—and by the truth of truth he is above what he mentioned and higher than what he described. How many a section containing eloquent high phrases and rare wonderful subtleties in assemblies we heard from him; how many a speculative problem we witnessed and saw him refuting opponents; how many a reminding session for the common people, serial in problems, filled with subtleties derived from fiqh problems, containing realities of usul, causing weeping in warning, causing joy in glad tidings, sealed with supplications and arts of intimate prayer we attended; how many a teaching gathering containing the senior imams and the presentation of problems to them and discussion of their depths we saw; and we acquired some of what we could from him and recorded it; and we did not appreciate what we were in, of the freshness of his days and the bloom of his months and years, its due measure; and we did not thank Allah Almighty for it due thanks until we lost him and were stripped of him.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is the author's own commentary following the quoted praise from al-Bakhrizi. Ibn Asakir asserts that the Imam is even greater than al-Bakhrizi described. He then enumerates various aspects of the Imam's activities he personally witnessed: eloquent speech in assemblies (fusul), debates in speculative theology (nazar), refutation of opponents (ifham al-khusum), preaching sessions for the common people that combined fiqh-derived subtleties, usul al-fiqh, emotional warnings and joyful tidings, all ending with prayers. Also teaching gatherings with senior imams. He collected some of his teachings but regrets not fully appreciating his blessings until after his death. The tone is one of loss and gratitude.

Misconception: The author is criticizing or undermining al-Bakhrizi's praise.
Correction: Actually, Ibn Asakir affirms it and adds his own higher estimation, saying the Imam is 'above what he mentioned and higher than what he described.'

Key Takeaway

The Imam's Devotion to Knowledge and Unconventional Habits
Description of the Imam's unusual sleeping and eating habits and his passion for learning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Imam's personal habits reflected his complete devotion to knowledge, and the testimony of a scholar who witnessed his humility and passion.

Core Idea
The Imam slept and ate only when compelled; his true nourishment was knowledge.

This passage gives us a rare glimpse into the daily life of a great scholar. We learn about his unusual sleeping and eating patterns and hear a firsthand account from a visiting grammarian who was amazed by the Imam's love for learning. The accounts reveal a man consumed by the pursuit of knowledge, to the point where even basic physical habits were secondary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the two separate anecdotes: the Imam's own words about his habits, and the story from al-Mujashi'i.
Notice the contrast between normal human habits and the Imam's irregular pattern.
2
Analyze the first anecdote
The Imam states that he does not sleep or eat out of habit, but only when forced by nature or desire. Reflect on what this says about his priorities.
His pleasure and recreation was in discussing and seeking knowledge.
3
Understand the second anecdote
Al-Mujashi'i, a grammarian, was received with honor and became the Imam's teacher, even though the Imam was the greatest imam of his time. How does this show humility?
The Imam carried the book he himself authored to study with a visiting scholar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسمعته في أثناء كلام يقول أنا لا أنام لا آكل عادة وإنما أنام إذا غلبني النوم ليلاً كان أو نهارا واكل إذا اشـتـهـيـت الطعام أى وقت كان ، كان لذته ولهوه وتنزهه فى مذاكرة العلم وطلب من أي نوع كان ، ولقد سمعت الشيخ أبا الحسن علی بن فضال بن على المجاشعى النحوى القادم علينا سنة تسع وستين وأربعمائة يقول : وقد قبله الإمام فخر الإسلام وقابله بالاكرام وأخذ فى قراءة النحو عليه والتلمذة له بعد إن كان إمام الأئمة فى وقته وكان يحمله كل يوم إلى داره يقرأ عليه کتاب ) أكسير الذهب في صناعة الأدب ( من تصنيفه فكان يحكى يوماً ويقول ما رأيت عاشقاً للعلم أى نوع كان مثل هذا الإمام فإنه يطلب العلم للعلم وكان كذلك.
And I heard him in the course of speech saying: I do not sleep, I do not eat by habit; rather I sleep only when sleep overcomes me, whether night or day, and I eat when I desire food, at any time. His pleasure, pastime, and recreation was in discussing knowledge and seeking it of any type. And I heard the Shaykh Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Faddal ibn Ali al-Mujashi'i al-Nahwi, who came to us in the year 469 AH, saying: And the Imam Fakhr al-Islam received him, welcomed him with honor, and began reading grammar with him and becoming his student, after he had been the imam of imams in his time. And he used to carry him every day to his house, and he would read to him the book 'Iksir al-Dhahab fi Sina'at al-Adab' from his own composition. And he narrated one day and said: I have not seen a lover of knowledge of any type like this Imam; indeed he seeks knowledge for the sake of knowledge. And he was like that.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage provides two personal accounts illustrating the Imam's character. First, the author reports hearing the Imam say that he does not have fixed habits for sleep and eating; he sleeps only when overpowered and eats only when he desires, at any time. His true pleasure and recreation was in discussing and seeking knowledge of all kinds. Second, the author relates a story from al-Mujashi'i, a grammarian who came to the city in 469 AH. When he arrived, the Imam (Fakhr al-Islam) received him with honor and began studying grammar with him as a student, despite being himself the greatest imam of his time. Al-Mujashi'i would daily be taken to the Imam's house to read a book composed by the Imam himself. The grammarian testified that he had never seen such a passionate lover of knowledge: the Imam sought knowledge purely for its own sake. This underscores the Imam's humility and pure intention.

Key Terms
فخر الإسلام
أبو الحسن المجاشعي
إكسير الذهب في صناعة الأدب
النحو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Imam's irregular eating and sleeping were signs of asceticism or disregard for physical needs.
Correction: The passage shows that his irregular habits were not due to asceticism but to his overwhelming love for knowledge, which made him prioritize it over routine.

Checkpoint

A) He seeks knowledge for fame
B) He seeks knowledge for the sake of knowledge
C) He seeks knowledge only for debate
D) He seeks knowledge to teach others

Key Takeaway
The Imam's life exemplified seeking knowledge purely for the sake of Allah, to the point where even basic human habits were shaped by this devotion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir

The Imam's Humility and Emotional Sensitivity
Enumerates the Imam's virtues of humility and tenderness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the remarkable humility and emotional sensitivity of the Imam as described in his biography.

Core Idea
True humility learns from everyone and weeps with every truth.

This passage reveals the inner character of the Imam: a man who never considered anyone too small to learn from, who attributed every benefit to its source, and who wept openly from tenderness of heart. These qualities are not signs of weakness but reflect his deep connection with truth and the prophetic example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Imam's virtues
Read the passage and list the specific virtues mentioned: never belittling anyone, attributing benefits, impartial criticism, extreme humility, weeping easily.
Note the paradox: his humility was so extreme it could be mistaken for mockery.
2
Reflect on the weeping in sessions
Notice how the Imam's tears during Sufi sessions moved others to tears. Consider why he wept—from burning within and realization of secret subtleties.
3
Address the common misunderstanding
Some might think extreme humility shows weakness. But the Imam's conduct follows the prophetic example of humility combined with strength in truth.
See the common_misunderstanding field.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن حميد سيرته أنه ما كان يستصغر أحداً حتى يسمع شادياً كان أو متناهياً فإن أصاب كياسة في طبع أو جرياً على منهاج الحقيقة استفاد منه صغيراً كان أو كبيرا ، ولا يستنكف أن الفائدة يعزى المستفادة إلى قائلها ويقول أن هذه الفائدة مما استفدته من فلان ، ولا يحابي أيضاً في التزييف إذا لم يرض كلاما ولو كان أباه أو أحداً من الأئمة المشهورين وكان من التواضع لكل أحد بمحل يتخيل منه الاستهزاء لمبالغته فيه ، ومن رقة القلب بحيث يبكي إذا سمع بيتاً أو تفكر في نفسه ساعة ، وإذا شرع في حكاية الأحوال وخاض في علوم الصوفية في فصول مجالسه بالغدوات أبكى الحاضرين ببكائه ، وقطر الدماء من الجفون بزعقاته ونعراته وإشاراته لاحتراقه فى نفسه وتحققه بما يجرى من دقائق الأسرار . هذه الجملة نبذ مما عهدنا منه إلى انتهاء أجله فادركه قضاء الله الذي لا بد منه بعد ما مرض قبل ذلك مرض اليرقان وبقى فيه أياما ثم برأ منه وعاد إلى الدرس والمجلس وأظهر الناس من الخواص والعوام السرور بصحته وإقباله من علته فبعد ذلك بعهد قريب مرض المرضة التي توفى منها وبقى فيها أياما وغلبت عليه الحرارة التي كانت تزور فى طبعه إلى أن ضعف وحمل إلى نستيقان لاعتدال الهواء وخفة الماء ، فزاد الضعف وبدت مخايل الموت وتوفى ليلة الأربعاء بعد صلاة العتمة الخامس والعشرين من شهر ربيع الآخر من سنة ثمان وسبعين وأربعمائة ، ونقل في الليلة إلى البلد وقام الصياح من كل جانب وجزع الفرق عليه جزعاً لم يعهد مثله ، وحمل بين الصلاتين من يوم الاربعاء إلى ميدان الحسين ولم تفتح الأبواب في البلد
And among his praiseworthy conduct was that he never considered anyone insignificant until he heard him, whether beginner or advanced. If he found cleverness in a temperament or adherence to the path of reality, he would benefit from him, whether young or old. And he was not too proud to attribute a benefit to its speaker and say: 'This benefit is something I obtained from so-and-so.' Nor did he show favoritism in refutation, if he was not pleased with a speech, even if it were his father or one of the famous imams. And he was of such humility towards everyone that one might imagine mockery due to its excess. And he was of such tenderness of heart that he would weep when hearing a verse or thinking about himself for an hour. And when he began narrating states (ahwal) and delved into the sciences of Sufism in his morning sessions, he would make the attendees weep with his weeping, and tears would drop from the eyes due to his shouts, cries, and gestures because of his burning within himself and his realization of what flows from the subtleties of secrets. This summary is a sample of what we witnessed from him until the end of his term. Then the decree of Allah, which is inevitable, overtook him after he had previously fallen ill with jaundice, remaining in it for days, then recovered and returned to teaching and sessions. The elite and common people showed joy at his health and his return from his illness. Then after a short time he fell ill with the illness from which he died, remaining in it for days. The fever that used to visit his nature overwhelmed him until he became weak and was carried to Nistiqan (?) for the moderate air and light water. But weakness increased and signs of death appeared. He died on the night of Wednesday after the night prayer, the 25th of Rabi' al-Akhir, 478 AH. He was moved that night to the city, and cries arose from every side, and the groups grieved over him with an unprecedented grief. He was carried between the two prayers on Wednesday to the Husayn Square, and the gates in the city were not opened.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a description of the Imam's excellent character and the account of his death. It first lists his virtues: He never belittled anyone, regardless of their level, and would learn from anyone if they showed intelligence or adherence to truth. He attributed benefits to their originators and was not biased in criticism, even against his father or famous imams. His humility was so extreme it could be mistaken for mockery. He was tender-hearted, weeping easily upon hearing a verse or reflecting. In Sufi-oriented morning sessions, he would weep and become emotionally intense, moving others to tears. Then the narrative shifts to his final illness. He first had jaundice, recovered, and resumed teaching, to the joy of the people. But soon after, he fell ill again with a fatal fever. He was taken to a place called 'Nistiqan' for its climate, but his condition worsened. He died on the given date. His body was brought to the city amid widespread grief. The funeral procession went to Husayn Square, and the city gates were not opened (perhaps due to crowds).

Key Terms

رقة القلب
علوم الصوفية
الأحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Extreme humility is a sign of weakness or lack of self-respect.
Correction: The Imam's humility is a prophetic trait, showing strength in following truth without ego.

Checkpoint
What did the Imam do when he heard a speaker he found clever?
A) He ignored him because he was young.
B) He benefited from him, regardless of age.
C) He criticized him for being a beginner.
D) He attributed the benefit to himself.

Key Takeaway
The Imam's humility and tenderness were rooted in his pursuit of truth and his deep spiritual realization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone speak, do you first judge their age or status, or do you genuinely listen for benefit? Commit to learning from one person today whom you would normally dismiss.
?
The Imam wept when hearing a verse or thinking about himself for an hour. When did you last weep over your own shortcomings before Allah? Set aside a quiet hour this week to reflect on your state and let your heart soften.
?
Are you impartial when receiving criticism, even from those you love? Recall a recent time you defended yourself out of pride. Resolve to accept a truthful criticism today without resentment.

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir

The Imam's Final Illness and Death
Narrates the sequence of the Imam's final illness and death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sequence of events leading to the Imam's death and the community's grief.

Core Idea
Even great souls taste death, and their departure leaves hearts aching.

This passage narrates the Imam's final days: a brief recovery from jaundice, then a fatal illness that weakened him. Despite being moved to a place with better climate, he passed away. His death caused unprecedented grief, and the city gates were closed in mourning.

Guided Path
1
Track the timeline of illnesses
First, the Imam suffered jaundice, recovered, and resumed teaching. Then a short while later, he fell ill again with a fever that eventually killed him.
Note the detail that people rejoiced at his recovery from jaundice.
2
Examine the place of death
He was moved to 'Nistiqan' for its moderate air and light water, but his weakness increased. This place name is uncertain.
The manuscript has 'نستيقان' which may be a misreading.
3
Observe the community's reaction
When he died, cries arose from all sides, the groups grieved intensely, and his body was carried to Husayn Square. The city gates were not opened, possibly due to the crowds or mourning.
The passage cuts off at 'ولم تفتح الأبواب'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir
beginner

The Life, Character, and Death of Imam Abu al-Ma'ali
Ibn Asakir's biographical account of Imam Abu al-Ma'ali, covering his character, death, and legacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the exemplary character of Imam Abu al-Ma'ali, the circumstances of his death, and the enduring impact of his scholarly legacy.

Core Idea
Imam Abu al-Ma'ali lived for knowledge and died mourned by all.

Ibn Asakir provides a heartfelt biography of Imam Abu al-Ma'ali, highlighting his unparalleled love for knowledge, his humility, and his deep spirituality. The passage then recounts his final illness, the unprecedented public mourning at his death, and the lasting influence of his scholarly works. Through this account, we glimpse the ideal of a scholar who embodied his learning.

Guided Path
1
Read the full biographical account
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overall sense of the Imam's life and legacy.
2
Identify the Imam's key character traits
Notice how Ibn Asakir describes his humility, eagerness to learn from anyone, and his emotional sensitivity.
3
Examine the details of his death and funeral
Pay attention to the illness, the date, and the dramatic mourning described by the author.
Note that he recovered from jaundice first.
4
Reflect on his scholarly heritage
Consider the list of teachers and the author's reflection that his knowledge endures beyond his lineage.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
له بعد إن كان إمام الأئمة فى وقته وكان يحمله كل يوم إلى داره يقرأ عليه كتاب ) أكسير الذهب في صناعة الأدب ( من تصنيفه فكان يحكى يوماً ويقول ما رأيت عاشقاً للعلم أى نوع كان مثل هذا الإمام فإنه يطلب العلم للعلم وكان كذلك. كلامه ومن حميد سيرته أنه ما كان يستصغر أحداً حتى يسمع شادياً كان أو متناهياً فإن أصاب كياسة في طبع أو جرياً على منهاج الحقيقة استفاد منه صغيراً كان أو كبيرا ، ولا يستنكف أن الفائدة يعزى المستفادة إلى قائلها ويقول أن هذه الفائدة مما استفدته من فلان ، ولا يحابي أيضاً في التزييف إذا لم يرض كلاما ولو كان أباه أو أحداً من الأئمة المشهورين وكان من التواضع لكل أحد بمحل يتخيل منه الاستهزاء لمبالغته فيه ، ومن رقة القلب بحيث يبكي إذا سمع بيتاً أو تفكر في نفسه ساعة ، وإذا شرع في حكاية الأحوال وخاض في علوم الصوفية في فصول مجالسه بالغدوات أبكى الحاضرين ببكائه ، وقطر الدماء من الجفون بزعقاته ونعراته وإشاراته لاحتراقه فى نفسه وتحققه بما يجرى من دقائق الأسرار . هذه الجملة نبذ مما عهدنا منه إلى انتهاء أجله فادركه قضاء الله الذي لا بد منه بعد ما مرض قبل ذلك مرض اليرقان وبقى فيه أياما ثم برأ منه وعاد إلى الدرس والمجلس وأظهر الناس من الخواص والعوام السرور بصحته وإقباله من علته فبعد ذلك بعهد قريب مرض المرضة التي توفى منها وبقى فيها أياما وغلبت عليه الحرارة التي كانت تزور فى طبعه إلى أن ضعف وحمل إلى نستيقان لاعتدال الهواء وخفة الماء ، فزاد الضعف وبدت مخايل الموت وتوفى ليلة الأربعاء بعد صلاة العتمة الخامس والعشرين من شهر ربيع الآخر من سنة ثمان وسبعين وأربعمائة ، ونقل في الليلة إلى البلد وقام الصياح من كل جانب وجزع الفرق عليه جزعاً لم يعهد مثله ، وحمل بين الصلاتين من يوم الاربعاء إلى ميدان الحسين ولم تفتح الأبواب في البلد ووضعت المناديل عن الرؤس عاما بحيث ما أجتراً أحد على ستر رأسه من الرؤس والكبار وصلى عليه ابنه الإمام أبو القسم بعد جهد جهيد حتى حمل إلى داره من شدة الزحمة وقت التطفيل ودفن في داره وبعد سنين نقل إلى مقبرة الحسين وكسر منبره في الجامع المنيعي وقعد الناس للعزاء أياما عزاء عاما ، وأكثر الشعراء المراثى فيه وكان الطلبة قريباً من أربعمائة نفر يطوفون في البلد نائحين عليه مكسرين المحابر والأقلام مبالغين في الصياح والجزع ، وكان مولده ثامن عشر المحرم سنة تسع عشرة وأربعمائة وتوفى وهو ابن تسع وخمسين سنة رحمه الله سمع الحديث الكثير في صباه من مشايخ مثل الشيخ أبي حسان وأبى سعد عليك وأبى سعد النضروى ومنصور بن رامش وجمع له كتاب الأربعين فسمعناه منه بقراءتي عليه وقد سمع سنن الدارقطني من أبى سعد ابن عليك وكان يعتمد تلك الأحاديث في مسائل الخلاف ويذكر الجرح والتعديل منها في الرواة وظنى أن آثار جده واجتهاده في دين الله يدوم إلى قيام الساعة وإن انقطع نسله من جهة الذكور ظاهراً فنشر علمه يقوم مقام كل نسب ، ويغنيه عن كل نشب مكتسب ، والله تعالى يسقى في كل لحظة جديدة تلك الروضة الشريفة عزالی رحمته ويزيد في ألطافه وكرامته بفضله ومنته إنه ولى كل خير . ومما قيل عند وفاته : قلوب العالمين على المقالي وأيام الورى شبه الليالي أيثمر غصن أهل الفضل يوما وقد مات الإمام أبو المعالي
For he was the Imam of Imams in his time, and he was carried every day to his house to read to him the book 'Iksir al-Dhahab fi Sina'at al-Adab' from his own authorship. He used to say one day, 'I have not seen a lover of knowledge, whatever type, like this Imam, for he seeks knowledge for knowledge's sake.' That was the case. His speech: Among his praiseworthy conduct was that he never belittled anyone, whether a beginner or an advanced student; if he found cleverness in a nature or adherence to the path of reality, he would benefit from it, whether the person was young or old. He was not too proud to attribute the benefit he had gained to its speaker, saying, 'This benefit I gained from so-and-so.' Nor did he show partiality in censuring if he was not pleased with a statement, even if it were his father or one of the famous imams. He was humble towards everyone to such an extent that one might imagine mockery due to its excess. He had such tenderness of heart that he would weep when he heard a verse or reflected on himself for an hour. When he began to narrate spiritual states and delve into the sciences of the Sufis in the sessions of his gatherings in the mornings, he would make the attendees weep with his weeping, and tears of blood would fall from eyes due to his cries, shouts, and gestures, because of his burning within himself and his realization of the subtle secrets that occurred. This is a summary of what we witnessed of him until the end of his life. Then the decree of Allah, which is inevitable, overtook him after he had previously suffered from jaundice, remained in it for some days, then recovered from it and returned to teaching and the gathering. The elite and common people showed joy at his health and his recovery from his illness. Shortly after that, he fell ill with the illness from which he died, remained in it for some days, and the heat that used to visit his nature overcame him until he became weak. He was carried to Nustiqan (?) for the moderate air and light water. Weakness increased and signs of death appeared. He died on the night of Wednesday after the night prayer, the 25th of Rabi' al-Akhir of the year 478 AH. He was transported that night to the city, and wailing rose from every side. The sects grieved for him with unprecedented grief. He was carried between the two prayers on Wednesday to the Maydan of al-Husayn. The doors of the city were not opened. Handkerchiefs were removed from heads generally, so that no one among the leaders and elders dared to cover his head. His son, Imam Abu al-Qasim, prayed over him after great effort until he was carried to his house due to the extreme crowding at the time of burial preparation. He was buried in his house, and after years he was transferred to the cemetery of al-Husayn. His pulpit in the al-Mani'i mosque was broken. People sat in mourning for days, a general mourning. Many poets composed elegies for him. About four hundred students roamed the city lamenting him, breaking inkwells and pens, exaggerating in wailing and grief. He was born on the 18th of Muharram of the year 419 AH and died at the age of fifty-nine, may Allah have mercy on him. He heard many hadith in his youth from teachers such as Shaykh Abu Hassan, Abu Sa'd 'Alik, Abu Sa'd al-Nadrawi, and Mansur ibn Ramish. He compiled the book 'al-Arba'in' and we heard it from him through my reading to him. He had heard the Sunan of al-Daraqutni from Abu Sa'd ibn 'Alik. He relied on those hadith in matters of juristic disagreement and mentioned jarh and ta'dil concerning the narrators. I think that the traces of his effort and diligence in the religion of Allah will endure until the Day of Resurrection, even if his lineage through males appears to be cut off. So the spreading of his knowledge takes the place of every lineage and suffices from every acquired wealth. May Allah, the Exalted, water that noble garden with abundant mercy every new moment and increase His favors and kindnesses to him by His bounty and grace. Verily, He is the Guardian of every good. Among what was said at his death: 'Hearts of the world are on griddles, and the days of people are like nights. Can the branch of virtuous people ever bear fruit when Imam Abu al-Ma'ali has died?'
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a detailed biographical account of an Imam (apparently Abu al-Ma'ali) by Ibn Asakir. It begins by describing his extraordinary love for knowledge: he was carried daily to study, and he remarked that this Imam sought knowledge purely for its own sake. Then it enumerates his virtuous qualities: he never looked down on anyone, whether beginner or advanced; he would benefit from anyone's cleverness, regardless of age; he attributed knowledge to its source rather than claiming it; he was impartial in criticism, even against his father; he was extremely humble, to the point of seeming ridiculous; he was tender-hearted, weeping at poetry or reflection; and his Sufi gatherings moved listeners to tears and ecstatic states. The narrative then turns to his final illness: he had jaundice, recovered, but then fell ill with a feverish sickness. He was taken to a place called Nustiqan for its moderate climate but his condition worsened. He died on a Wednesday night, 25 Rabi' al-Akhir 478 AH. The city was in uproar; doors were closed, heads uncovered in mourning. His son led the funeral prayer amid extreme crowding. He was initially buried in his home, later moved to the Husayn cemetery. His pulpit was broken, and four hundred students mourned dramatically, breaking inkwells and pens. The author gives his birth date (18 Muharram 419 AH) and age at death (59). He then lists his hadith teachers: Abu Hassan, Abu Sa'd 'Alik, Abu Sa'd al-Nadrawi, and Mansur ibn Ramish. He compiled 'al-Arba'in' and studied the Sunan of al-Daraqutni. The author reflects that his scholarly legacy will endure even if his male line ends. The passage concludes with an elegiac couplet mourning his death.

Key Terms

نستيقان
ميدان الحسين
الأربعين
سنن الدارقطني
الجرح والتعديل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imam Abu al-Ma'ali died of jaundice.
Correction: He recovered from jaundice and later died from a different feverish illness.

Checkpoint

B) A feverish illness with heat

Key Takeaway
Imam Abu al-Ma'ali's life exemplified the pursuit of knowledge for its own sake, and his legacy of learning continues beyond his physical lineage.

?

?
Would you have the courage to reject falsehood if it came from your own father or a respected authority? Examine your heart: are you more afraid of losing relationships than of displeasing Allah?

ومنهم الفقيه أبو الفتح نصر بن إبراهيم المقدسی رحمه الله متأخر الوفاة أدركنا جماعة ممن أدركه وتفقه به ، وكان قد تفقه عند أبى الفتح سليم بن أيوب الرازى بصور ثم رحل إلى ديار بكر ، وتفقه عند أبي عبد الله محمد بن بيان الكازروني الفقيه وسمع الحديث بدمشق وغيرها من جماعة ودرس العلم ببيت المقدس مدة ثم انتقل إلى صور فأقام

He heard hadith in Damascus and other places from a group. He taught knowledge in Bayt al-Maqdis (Jerusalem) for a period, then moved to Sur and resided there.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a brief biographical notice of another scholar, Abu al-Fath Nasr ibn Ibrahim al-Maqdisi, who died later than the main subject. Ibn Asakir notes that he met people who studied with him. He then recounts Abu al-Fath's educational journey: he first studied jurisprudence under Abu al-Fath Sulaym ibn Ayyub al-Razi in Sur (Tyre), then traveled to Diyar Bakr (upper Mesopotamia) to study under Abu 'Abd Allah Muhammad ibn Bayan al-Kazaruni. He also heard hadith in Damascus and other places. He taught in Jerusalem for a time before moving back to Sur, where he settled. The text ends mid-sentence with 'and resided there,' but the available excerpt concludes there.

Biography of Imam Abu al-Ma'ali (al-Juwayni)
Describes the funeral, birth, death, teachers, and legacy of Imam Abu al-Ma'ali

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the life, death, and enduring legacy of Imam Abu al-Ma'ali al-Juwayni as described by Ibn Asakir.

Core Idea
A scholar's knowledge outlasts his lineage and becomes his true legacy.

This passage from Ibn Asakir's Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari provides a vivid account of the death and mourning of Imam Abu al-Ma'ali (al-Juwayni), along with details of his birth, teachers, and contributions. It emphasizes the profound impact of his scholarship, suggesting that his knowledge endures beyond any earthly lineage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get a sense of the narrative flow. Notice the vivid descriptions of mourning and the list of teachers.
2
Understand the mourning scene
Reflect on details like the removal of head coverings, breaking of the pulpit, and students breaking inkwells. These were cultural expressions of extreme grief.
This shows the high esteem in which he was held.
3
Identify his teachers and works
Find the names of his teachers and the books he composed or heard: al-Arba'in and Sunan al-Daraqutni. Consider how these contributed to his legacy.
4
Grasp the concept of legacy
Ibn Asakir states that even if his male lineage ends, his knowledge takes its place. This is a powerful statement about the immortality of scholarship.
This connects to the core idea.
5
Reflect on the poem
Read the elegy at the end. It expresses the world's grief at his loss. Consider how this poetic summary encapsulates his impact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووضعت المناديل عن الرؤس عاما بحيث ما أجتراً أحد على ستر رأسه من الرؤس والكبار وصلى عليه ابنه الإمام أبو القسم بعد جهد جهيد حتى حمل إلى داره من شدة الزحمة وقت التطفيل ودفن في داره وبعد سنين نقل إلى مقبرة الحسين وكسر منبره في الجامع المنيعي وقعد الناس للعزاء أياما عزاء عاما ، وأكثر الشعراء المراثى فيه وكان الطلبة قريباً من أربعمائة نفر يطوفون في البلد نائحين عليه مكسرين المحابر والأقلام مبالغين في الصياح والجزع ، وكان مولده ثامن عشر المحرم سنة تسع عشرة وأربعمائة وتوفى وهو ابن تسع وخمسين سنة رحمه الله
سمع الحديث الكثير في صباه من مشايخ مثل الشيخ أبي حسان وأبى سعد عليك وأبى سعد النضروى ومنصور بن رامش وجمع له كتاب الأربعين فسمعناه منه بقراءتي عليه وقد سمع سنن الدارقطني من أبى سعد ابن عليك وكان يعتمد تلك الأحاديث في مسائل الخلاف ويذكر الجرح والتعديل منها في الرواة وظنى أن آثار جده واجتهاده في دين الله يدوم إلى قيام الساعة وإن انقطع نسله من جهة الذكور ظاهراً فنشر علمه يقوم مقام كل نسب ، ويغنيه عن كل نشب مكتسب ، والله تعالى يسقى في كل لحظة جديدة تلك الروضة الشريفة عزالی رحمته ويزيد في ألطافه وكرامته بفضله ومنته إنه ولى كل خير . ومما قيل عند وفاته :
قلوب العالمين على المقالي وأيام الورى شبه الليالي أيثمر غصن أهل الفضل يوما وقد مات الإمام أبو المعالي
And the head coverings were removed from the heads (publicly) so that no one dared to cover his head from among the chiefs and elders. His son, Imam Abu al-Qasim, prayed over him after great difficulty until he was carried to his house due to the extreme crowding and the funeral procession time. He was buried in his house, and after years he was transferred to the cemetery of al-Hussain. His pulpit was broken in the Mani'i mosque, and the people sat for mourning for days, a public mourning. And the poets composed many elegies for him, and there were nearly four hundred students wandering in the city lamenting him, breaking inkwells and pens, exaggerating in shouting and distress. His birth was on the 18th of Muharram, in the year 419 AH, and he died at the age of fifty-nine, may Allah have mercy on him. He heard many hadith in his youth from teachers such as Shaykh Abu Hassan, Abu Sa'd Alayk, Abu Sa'd al-Nadrawi, and Mansur ibn Ramish. He compiled for him the book 'al-Arba'in' (The Forty), so we heard it from him through my recitation to him. He also heard the Sunan of al-Daraqutni from Abu Sa'd ibn Alayk, and he relied on those hadith in issues of legal disagreement and mentioned jarh and ta'dil from them in the narrators. I think that the effects of his effort and his diligence in the religion of Allah will continue until the Day of Resurrection, even if his offspring from males apparently ceases. So the spreading of his knowledge takes the place of every lineage and suffices him from every acquired wealth. And Allah Almighty waters that noble garden with His mercy every new moment and increases His favors and honors upon it by His bounty and grace. He is the Guardian of every good. And among what was said at his death: 'The hearts of the worlds are on the frying pans, and the days of people are like nights. Will the branch of virtuous people ever bear fruit when the Imam Abu al-Ma'ali has died?'
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a biographical sketch of Imam Abu al-Ma'ali (al-Juwayni) focusing on his death, mourning, teachers, and legacy. It begins by describing the extraordinary public mourning: head coverings were removed, no one dared to cover his head, reflecting the severity of grief. His son led the funeral prayer with difficulty due to the massive crowd. Initially buried at home, later his remains were moved to the cemetery of al-Hussain. Official signs of mourning included breaking his pulpit in the mosque. Students, numbering about four hundred, wandered the city wailing, breaking inkwells and pens in exaggerated sorrow. Then it gives his birth date (18 Muharram 419 AH) and death at age 59. Next, it lists hadith scholars from whom he heard in his youth, including Shaykh Abu Hassan, Abu Sa'd Alayk, Abu Sa'd al-Nadrawi, and Mansur ibn Ramish. He authored 'al-Arba'in' (The Forty Hadith), which the author (Ibn Asakir) heard from him by recitation. He also heard al-Daraqutni's Sunan from Abu Sa'd ibn Alayk and used those hadith in legal discussions, identifying jarh and ta'dil in narrators. The author then expresses his belief that the scholar's efforts in religion will endure until Judgment Day, even if his male lineage ends, because his knowledge suffices as lineage and wealth. He prays for divine mercy upon his grave. The passage concludes with two lines of elegy poetry mourning his death.

سنن الدارقطني
أبو المعالي
الجرح والتعديل
المناديل
المحابر والأقلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Breaking inkwells and pens was a sign of disrespect to the scholar.
Correction: It was actually a traditional expression of extreme grief for a scholar, showing the depth of loss and the high esteem in which he was held.

Checkpoint

D) His books were lost after his death.

Key Takeaway
Imam Abu al-Ma'ali's knowledge became his eternal lineage, surpassing worldly riches and ensuring his legacy until the Day of Resurrection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your life ended today, what specific 'knowledge' or impact would you leave that would outlast you? This lesson shows that Imam al-Juwayni's knowledge became his eternal lineage. What are you actively investing in right now to build such a legacy, and why aren't you doing more?
?
The students broke their inkwells and pens in grief over his death. How deep is your emotional connection to the scholars of Islam? When you hear of a scholar's passing, does it drive you to re-evaluate your own pursuit of knowledge, or does it pass unnoticed? What would it take for you to love and honor scholars with that intensity?
?
The author claims that spreading knowledge replaces every lineage and suffices from wealth. If that is true, what have you personally sacrificed—time, comfort, reputation—for the sake of learning and teaching the deen? If nothing, what does that say about your priorities in light of this eternal perspective?

Early Life and Asceticism of Abu al-Fath
Ibn Asakir details the early life, education, and ascetic lifestyle of Abu al-Fath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early life, teachers, and ascetic practices of the jurist Abu al-Fath Nasr ibn Ibrahim.

Core Idea
Abu al-Fath embodied the asceticism of the early Salaf through his dedication to knowledge and detachment from worldly rulers.

This passage from Ibn Asakir's Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari introduces us to the jurist Abu al-Fath, a scholar known for his deep learning and extreme piety. We learn about his teachers, his journey in seeking knowledge, and his unwavering commitment to the path of the Salaf, characterized by austerity and independence from rulers. This lesson will focus on these foundational aspects of his life.

Guided Path
1
Read the biographical introduction
Read the opening lines that state who Abu al-Fath is and mention his teachers.
Notice the chain of learning: he studied under specific scholars.
2
Identify his academic career
Trace his movements: Jerusalem, Tyre, Damascus, and note the duration and activities in each place.
He spent ten years in Tyre despite opposition.
3
Focus on his ascetic practices
Read the description of his lifestyle: austerity, avoiding rulers, reliance on a small land produce.
This is the core of his character.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم الفقيه أبو الفتح نصر بن إبراهيم المقدسی رحمه الله متأخر الوفاة أدركنا جماعة ممن أدركه وتفقه به ، وكان قد تفقه عند أبى الفتح سليم بن أيوب الرازى بصور ثم رحل إلى ديار بكر ، وتفقه عند أبي عبد الله محمد بن بيان الكازروني الفقيه وسمع الحديث بدمشق وغيرها من جماعة ودرس العلم ببيت المقدس مدة ثم انتقل إلى صور فأقام بها عشر سنين ينشر العلم بها مع كثرة المخالفين له من الرافضة ، ثم أنتقل منها إلى دمشق فأقام بها تسع سنين يحدث ويدرس ويفتى على طريقة واحدة من الزهد في الدنيا والتنزه عن الدنايا والجرى على منهاج السلف من التقشف وتجنب السلاطين ورفع الطمع والاجتزاء باليسير مما يصل إليه من غلة أرض كانت له بنابلس يأتيه منها ما يقتاته ولا يقبل من أحد شيئا . سمعت من يحكى أن تاج الدولة تتش بن البارسلان زاره يوماً فلم يقم له وسأله عن أحل الأموال التي يتصرف فيها السلطان فقال الفقيه أحلها أموال الجزية ، فخرج من عنده وأرسل إليه بمبلغ من المال وقال هذا من مال الجزية ففرقه على الأصحاب ولم يقبله وقال لا حاجة بنا إليه فلما ذهب الرسول لامه الفقيه أبو الفتح نصر الله بن محمد وقال له قد علمت حاجتنا إليه فلو كنت قبلته وفرقته فينا ، فقال له لا تجزع من فوته فسوف يأتيك من الدنيا ما يكفيك فيما بعد ، فكان كما تفرس فيه رحمه الله . وسمعت بعض من صحبه يقول لو كان الفقيه أبو الفتح في السلف لم تقصر درجته عن واحد منهم لكنهم فاتوه بالسبق ، وكانت أوقاته كلها مستغرقة في عمل الخير إما في نشر علم وإما في إصلاح عمل ، وحكى عن بعض أهل العلم أنه قال صحبت إمام الحرمين أبا المعالي الجويني بخراسان ثم قدمت العراق فصحبت الشيخ أبا إسحق الشيرازى فكانت طريقته عندی أفضل من طريقة أبى المعالى ثم قدمت الشام فرأيت الفقيه أبا الفتح فكانت طريقته أحسن من طريقتيهما جميعا . سمعت الشيخ الفقيه أبا الفتح نصر الله بن محمد بن عبد القوى المصيصى يقول توفي الفقيه أبو الفتح نصر بن إبراهيم فى يوم الثلاثاء التاسع من المحرم سنة تسعين وأربعمائة بدمشق وخرجنا بجنازته بعد صلاة الظهر فلم يمكننا دفنه إلى قريب المغرب لأن الناس حالوا بيننا وبينه وكان الخلق متوافرا ، ذكر الدمشقيون أنهم لم يروا جنازة مثلها وأقمنا على قبره سبع ليال نقرأ كل ليلة عشرين ختمة رحمه الله ونضر وجهه
And among them is the jurist Abu al-Fath Nasr ibn Ibrahim al-Maqdisi, may Allah have mercy on him, whose death was later. We encountered a group who met him and studied fiqh with him. He had studied fiqh under Abu al-Fath Sulaym ibn Ayyub al-Razi in Tyre, then traveled to Diyar Bakr, and studied under Abu Abd Allah Muhammad ibn Bayan al-Kazaruni the jurist. He heard hadith in Damascus and elsewhere from a group, and taught knowledge in Jerusalem for a while, then moved to Tyre and stayed there for ten years spreading knowledge, despite many opponents from the Rafida. Then he moved from there to Damascus and stayed there for nine years, narrating hadith, teaching, and issuing legal verdicts in one consistent manner of asceticism in the world, abstaining from base things, adhering to the path of the Salaf in austerity, avoiding rulers, abandoning greed, and being content with little from the produce of a land he owned in Nablus from which he got his sustenance, and he would not accept anything from anyone. I heard someone relate that Taj al-Dawla Tutush ibn al-Barsalan visited him one day, and he did not stand up for him. He asked him about the most lawful wealth that the sultan can use. The jurist said: 'The most lawful is the wealth from jizya.' So he left from his presence and sent him an amount of money saying: 'This is from jizya wealth.' He distributed it among his companions and did not accept it, saying: 'We have no need of it.' When the messenger left, the jurist Abu al-Fath Nasrallah ibn Muhammad reproached him and said: 'You know we need it; why did you not accept and distribute it among us?' He replied: 'Do not grieve over losing it, for sufficient worldly provision will come to you later.' And it happened as he foresaw, may Allah have mercy on him. And I heard some of his companions say: 'If the jurist Abu al-Fath had been among the Salaf, his rank would not fall short of any of them, but they preceded him in time.' His whole time was absorbed in doing good: either spreading knowledge or correcting actions. And it was narrated from some scholars that he said: 'I accompanied Imam al-Haramayn Abu al-Ma'ali al-Juwayni in Khurasan, then I came to Iraq and accompanied Shaykh Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi, and I considered his way better than the way of Abu al-Ma'ali. Then I came to Syria and saw the jurist Abu al-Fath, and his way was better than both of them together.' I heard the shaykh and jurist Abu al-Fath Nasrallah ibn Muhammad ibn Abd al-Qawi al-Masisi say: 'The jurist Abu al-Fath Nasr ibn Ibrahim died on Tuesday, the 9th of Muharram, in the year 490 AH, in Damascus. We brought out his janazah after Zuhr prayer, but we could not bury him until near Maghrib because people hindered us from him, and the crowd was immense. The Damascenes mentioned that they had never seen a janazah like it. We stayed at his grave for seven nights, reciting twenty completions of the Qur'an each night. May Allah have mercy on him and brighten his face.'
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of the jurist Abu al-Fath Nasr ibn Ibrahim al-Maqdisi. It begins by noting that he died later, and the author (Ibn Asakir) encountered people who studied under him. Then it traces his education: he studied fiqh under Abu al-Fath Sulaym ibn Ayyub al-Razi in Tyre, then traveled to Diyar Bakr, and studied under Abu Abd Allah Muhammad ibn Bayan al-Kazaruni. He also heard hadith in Damascus and elsewhere from various teachers, and taught in Jerusalem for a time. He then moved to Tyre for ten years, spreading knowledge despite opposition from the Rafida (a term for Shi'a groups). After that, he moved to Damascus for nine years, engaged in teaching and issuing fatwas, characterized by asceticism, abstention from base matters, adherence to the Salafi path of austerity, avoiding rulers, renouncing greed, and being content with the meager produce from land in Nablus. He accepted nothing from anyone. An anecdote illustrates his piety: when ruler Taj al-Dawla Tutush visited, Abu al-Fath did not stand for him, and when asked about the most lawful wealth for a sultan, he replied jizya. The ruler sent him money as jizya, but he distributed it to companions and refused personal share. When a colleague reproached him for not accepting for others, he predicted that sufficient provision would come, which proved true. Another testimony says that had he been among the Salaf, he would have equaled them, but they preceded him. His time was wholly devoted to good works. A scholar who had accompanied al-Juwayni and al-Shirazi declared that Abu al-Fath's way surpassed both. The account ends with his death on Tuesday, 9th Muharram 490 AH in Damascus, with an enormous funeral procession that delayed burial, and seven nights of Qur'an recitation at his grave.

Key Terms
أبو الفتح نصر بن إبراهيم
الرافضة
التقشف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the term 'Rafida' is a generic insult for all Shi'a
Correction: Historically, 'Rafida' referred specifically to early Shi'a who rejected the first two caliphs, though later usage varied.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Fath's life exemplified the Salafi ideal of combining deep scholarship with extreme asceticism and detachment from rulers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Abu al-Fath avoided rulers and accepted nothing from anyone, instead living off his own land. Consider your own attachment to status and money. Where in your life do you seek validation from powerful people or material symbols? What would it take for you to be truly independent like him, trusting only in Allah's provision? What is one step you can take this week to reduce your dependence on the approval of others?
?
He spent ten years in Tyre teaching despite many opponents from the Rafida. Think about a situation where you have faced opposition for your beliefs. Did you persist like Abu al-Fath, or did you retreat? Write down one conviction you have been quiet about because of fear. Commit to speaking about it with someone this week.
?
He was content with little from his land. Take a moment to list the things you consider 'necessities' that might actually be excess. Are you willing to let go of one of them to practice contentment? If not, what is holding you back?

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir
beginner

The Sultan's Visit and the Death of Abu al-Fath
Ibn Asakir recounts the encounter with Sultan Tutush and the details of Abu al-Fath's death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the famous encounter between Abu al-Fath and Sultan Tutush, and the remarkable events surrounding his death.

Core Idea
Abu al-Fath's refusal to honor the ruler and his prediction of provision demonstrate his spiritual authority and trust in Allah.

The passage continues with a striking story: the ruler Taj al-Dawla Tutush visits Abu al-Fath, and the scholar not only refrains from standing but also gives a bold answer about lawful wealth. The ruler sends him jizya money, which he distributes but does not accept personally, and he even prophesies that sufficient provision will come. The passage concludes with tributes from contemporaries and the extraordinary funeral and posthumous Qur'an recitations.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote with the sultan
Read how the sultan visited, the question about lawful wealth, and the scholar's response.
Notice that Abu al-Fath did not stand up for the sultan.
2
Analyze the money incident
The sultan sends jizya money, but Abu al-Fath distributes it to companions and refuses personal share.
Also note the reproach from a fellow scholar and Abu al-Fath's prediction.
3
Read the testimonies about his stature
Read the statement that he would equal the Salaf, and the scholar who compared him favorably to al-Juwayni and al-Shirazi.
These show the high regard of his peers.
4
Read about his death and funeral
Read the date, the delay due to crowd, and the seven nights of Qur'an recitation.
This highlights the public love for him.
